<div class="word-content">
<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>THE
CONVERSATIONAL VERSION</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:center'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>TRANSLATION
NOTES FOR THE NEW TESTAMENT </span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Virgil
Warren, PhD</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>     
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            <i>The
Conversational Version</i> falls between wooden translation and loose paraphrase.
It is not a word-for-word translation or one told “in our own words.” Its main
contribution is at the second level of translation, the custom-of-usage level, that
is, semantic equivalence. The aim has been to use a casual, everyday manner of
speaking. The translation tries to follow specific patterns of form and grammar
that do not affect subject matter. That includes several standard principles of
written composition:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>        *a.
transforming passive clauses and phrases to active ones </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>          b.  changing
sequences in lists to make them natural and to fit English conventions</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>          c.  replacing
negative statements with equivalent positive statements</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>          d.
preferring action-verb wording over linking-verb constructions</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>        *e.  negating
the verb rather than the direct object</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>         *f.  eliminating
unnecessary conjunctions between clauses and in lists </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>        *g.  using
“pro-verbs,” contractions, and other “shorts”</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>          h.  eliminating
unnecessarily repetitious statements</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>           i.  using
restrained language</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>           j.  using
words and expressions common in current conversation</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>        *k.  replacing
participles with finite verbs</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>         *l.  replacing
“of” constructions with more specific connections to the phrase head</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:45.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>       *m.  avoiding
pendens constructions, historical presents, futuristic presents, timeless
aorists, imperatival futures, third-person imperatives, absolute constructions,
and other less customary English verbal usages</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:45.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>        *n.  deleting
“body-part” language, litotes, and other idioms less customary in English</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:45.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>          o.  establishing
continuity of person, tense, number, and viewpoint</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:45.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>        *p.  shortening
long sentences fostered by Greek and Hebrew syntax as created by the pleonastic
expression, tautology, redundancy, conjunctive elements, and the general verbosity
of Hebrew idiom compared to English </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:45.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Symbols</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            words     original
Greek authors’ words translated </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>word</span><sup><span
style='font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>p</span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>      (a)
<u>h</u>istoric <u>p</u>resent in the Greek; vivid narration. Many of these
instances drop out altogether in <i>CV </i>because<i> </i>they appear on the
present of </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>λέγω</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> in seemingly unnecessary
introductions to direct quotations.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           (b)
futuristic present in the Greek (John <b>7</b>:52; Philippians <b>1</b>:17-18)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           (c)
aoristic or perfective present in the Greek (Luke <b>15</b>:29)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word</span><sup><span
style='font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>f</span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>      imperatival
future</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word<sup>sg
</sup>    unclear word that is singular; particularly with the word <i>you</i>
and with command forms</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word<sup>pl       </sup>unclear
word that is plural</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word<sup>mas</sup>  masculine
reference </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word<sup>fem</sup>   feminine
reference</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word<sup>lit
</sup>    significantly interpreted expression</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            </span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>words”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> dialog
and quotations by the original authors </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            “words”  words
from quoted material that appear in the New Testament author’s comments: very
frequently as in Hebrews: <b>2</b>:5-14; <b>3</b>:13-<b>4</b>:6; <b>6</b>:20-<b>7</b>:3,
25; <b>8</b>:13; <b>10</b>:10, <i>etc.</i> cp. 1 Peter <b>5</b>:6. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            ‘word’    words
used in adapted ways, including bending the terminology of one system to label
the corresponding feature of an alternative system, instances like </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           ‘Babylon’
(1 Peter <b>5</b>:13; Revelation <b>14</b>:8; <b>16</b>:19; <b>17</b>:5; <b>18</b>:2,
10, 21),</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           ‘eunuch’
(Matthew<b> 19</b>:12), ‘Israel’ (Galatians <b>6:</b>16), ‘wisdom’ (James <b>3</b>:15),</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           ‘wealth’
(James <b>5</b>:3), ‘dress’ (1 Timothy <b>2</b>:10), ‘Father’ (<b>1</b>:16; <b>8</b>:5),
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           ‘slave’
(<b>6</b>:15, 16, 18, 22; 1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:22; ‘free’ (Romans <b>6</b>:20),</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:1.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  ‘law’
(Romans <b>3</b>:27<sup>3</sup>; 1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:21; Galatians <b>6</b>:2;
1 Timothy <b>2</b>:8; James <b>1</b>:24; <b>2</b>:12), </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:1.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  ‘circumcision’
(Philippians <b>3</b>:2, 23; Colossians <b>2</b>:11<sup>2</sup>; </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:1.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  ‘dead’
(Ephesians <b>2</b>:1, 5;<b> 2</b>:5; Colossians <b>2</b>:13), </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:1.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  ‘make
alive’ (Ephesians <b>2</b>:5; Colossians <b>2</b>:13)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            <i>words</i>     translator’s
added words for clarification in non-dialog material </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            <b><i>words
    </i></b>Old Testament quotations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            <u>words</u>     translator’s
addition for highlighting lists and for suggested stress in reading</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            [words]   translator’s
occasional explanatory notation within the text: Matthew <b>2</b>:23;
Revelation <b>6</b>:15; <b>9</b>:7, 9, 11; <b>21</b>:20<sup>2</sup></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            [<sup>444</sup>]<sup>ms</sup>
    original standard English verses omitted by the <i>UBS</i> 5<sup>th</sup>
rev ed based on manuscript evidence </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Matthew<b>
6</b>:13b (conclusion to the Model Prayer); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>18</b>:11
(<i>“the Son of Man came to save sinners”</i>); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>23</b>:14
(woe for devouring widows houses); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:1.75in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.75in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Mark<b>
7</b>:16 (<i>“if anybody has ears, let him hear”</i>); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:2.0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.75in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                          <b>9</b>:44
or 46 (<i>“where their worm doesn’t die, and the fire is not quenched”</i>); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:2.0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.75in'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                          11</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:26
(<i>“But if you don’t forgive, your Father in heaven won’t forgive your
transgressions”</i>; cp. Matthew <b>6</b>:15; <b>18</b>:35);</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>15</b>:28
(scripture fulfilled, <i>“He was numbered with the </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                transgressors”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>);
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Luke
<b>9</b>:55b (<i>“You don’t know what mentality you’re supposed to have.”</i>);</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>17</b>:36
(two in the field; one taken/left); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>23</b>:17
(Pilate’s releasing a prisoner at the feast); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    John<b>
5</b>:4 (troubling of the waters); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Acts
<b>8</b>:37 (<i>“if you believe, you may,”</i> <i>etc.</i>); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>15</b>:34
(Silas stays in Antioch); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>24</b>:6b-8a
(additional accusations against Paul before Felix); </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <b>28:</b>29
(the Jews’ leaving Paul while disputing among themselves;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Romans<b>
16</b>:24 (<i>“The grace of the Lord Jesus Christ be with you all. </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                Amen.”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    1
John <b>3</b>:8 (“the three witnesses passage”; re-versified in translations </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                later
than the King James Version). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                           All
the omitted verses (except Romans <b>16</b>:24 and 1 John <b>3</b>:8) are in
the historical books of the New Testament—about fifteen texts.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            [words]<sup>ms</sup> <u>parts
of verses</u> textual critics question but leave in the text: Matthew <b>6</b>:4,
6, 13b*; <b>12</b>:15; <b>22</b>:35; <b>27</b>:16, 17, 35b (fulfilled Psalm<b>
22</b>:19), 49b; Mark <b>3</b>:32; <b>7</b>:4; <b>11</b>:26; Luke <b>11</b>:33;<b>
13</b>:35; <b>17</b>:3; <b>22</b>:43-44*; <b>23</b>:34*; <b>24</b>:32, 52; John<b>
6</b>:64; 1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:29; 2 Corinthians <b>11</b>:3; Ephesians <b>1</b>:1*;
Colossians <b>3</b>:6; 2 Thessalonians<b> 1</b>:2; <b>3</b>:6; 1 Timothy<b> 6</b>:13;
Hebrews <b>13</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>[<sup>x</sup>]<sup>repeat
</sup>or [ ]<sup> </sup><i>CV</i> omits a few standard English verses as
repetitive or unnecessary: John <b>9</b>:23; Ephesians <b>4</b>:21 (repeat);
Matthew <b>24</b>:25; 2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:3; <b>12</b>:3; 1 John <b>2</b>:13b</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><b><sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>x-x</span></sup></b><sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                 +</span></sup><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>CV</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
combines some verses: Matthew<b> 7</b>:17-18; Mark <b>7</b>:8-9; Luke <b>10</b>:22-23;
John <b>19</b>:19-20; Acts <b>1</b>:21-22; <b>10</b>:39-40; Romans <b>1</b>:3-5,
14-15; <b>9</b>:1-2, 11-12; <b>15</b>:22-23; 1 Corinthians <b>4</b>:12-13; <b>16</b>:15-16;
Galatians <b>1</b>:11-12; Ephesians <b>1</b>:22-23; <b>2</b>:11-12; 14-15; <b>5</b>:8-9;
<b>6</b>:2-3; Philemon 15-16; Hebrews <b>7</b>:20-21; <b>13</b>:20-21; 1 Peter <b>3</b>:15-16;
1 John <b>1</b>:8-10; Revelation <b>1</b>:5-6; <b>2</b>:22-23. Greek has
number-gender-case endings to help tie words together. English has only
position for that purpose. To create appropriate connections, sometimes we have
lifted elements from farther along in long constructions. so they fit together
better for English purposes.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-45.0pt'><b><sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>x,
</span></sup></b><sup><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>z, y             </span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>In
Revelation <b>21</b>:24, 26, 25 <i>CV</i> re-sequences verses to clarify the connections;
see also John <b>2</b>:14.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            (words)   original
authors’ comments parenthesized by Greek text editors or in typical
translations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            <i>[words]</i>  original
authors’ comments inside quoted material; we have bracketed what Greek text editors
parenthesized.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            [[words]]
manuscript material highly questioned by textual critics but retained in the <i>UBS</i></span>
<span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>text:
Matthew <b>17</b>:21; <b>21</b>:44; Mark <b>16</b>:9-12; Luke <b>22</b>:43-44
(angels helped him; he sweat drops of blood); <b>23</b>:34a (<i>“forgive them;
they don’t know what they’re doing”</i>); John<b> 7</b>:53-<b>8</b>:11. About a
half dozen texts. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            LXX       distinctive
Septuagint wording: Matthew <b>15</b>:9; <b>20</b>:16; Mark <b>7</b>:7; Acts <b>8</b>:33;
<b>13</b>:34, 35, 41; Romans <b>10</b>:21; Hebrews <b>2</b>:8, 13; <b>13</b>:21;
James <b>4</b>:6; 1 Peter <b>4</b>:18; <b>5</b>:5 as per <i>UBS 5<sup>th</sup>
rev ed</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            !?            punctuation
used exclusively for several deliberative questions</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            word<sup>lit</sup>     significant
rewording from the <u>lit</u>eral original: Matthew <b>12</b>:8; Mark <b>2</b>:28;
Luke <b>7</b>:35; <b>17</b>:21; <b>24</b>:16, 31</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            </span><sup><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>+</span></sup><sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                    </span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>complete
list in these notations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *             major
point in these notations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:81.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Writing
the way American English speakers talk informally does not amount to aiming for
a certain “grade level” vocabulary, although the endeavor tends to have that
effect. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            We
have not followed the principle of translating the same Greek word with the
same English word each time. That ideal takes second place to letting different
contexts nuance Greek terms in ways that English’s closest equivalents cannot match.
Similar terms across different languages seldom correspond one-for-one. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Likewise,
we have not necessarily been able to translate the same Old Testament citation
the same way each time. A prime example occurs with Isaiah <b>6</b>:9-10, which
the New Testament cites five times: <sup>+</sup>Matthew <b>13</b>:14-15 = Mark <b>4</b>:123
= Luke <b>8</b>:10; John <b>12</b>:39; Acts <b>28</b>:27. The quotations vary
as references to the “sense” of the original text that the New Testament writer
wants to draw on. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            The
New Testament wording of Old Testament texts is also different from the
original Hebrew because New Testament writers usually follow the Septuagint
Greek translation (LXX), which introduces some differences from the original standardized
Hebrew text. Besides, they tend to word the citations in a way that fits their
immediate application of the reference. Their interest may be more in the sense
of the text and its implications than in the wording itself. Recitation from
memory may have come into play as well.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            A
broader illustration of the point derives from Isaiah <b>56</b>:7, <i>“Because
my house will be called a house of prayer for all the peoples.”</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        In
Matthew<b> 21</b>:13 <i>CV</i> frames the idea in command form, <i>“Call my
house a house for prayer”; for “My house shall be called a house of prayer”</i>
(<i>NASB</i>). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Mark<b>
11</b>:17 reads in <i>CV</i>, <i>“Call my house a house of prayer for all the
nations”</i>; for the literal <i>“My house will be called a house of prayer for
all the nations.”</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Luke<b>
19</b>:46 in <i>CV</i> says, <i>“My house will be a house for prayer”</i> for <i>“My
house will be a house of prayer”</i> (lit).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Our
wording of Isaiah<b> 56</b>:8 (<i>CV</i>) makes the Hebrew into (1) an
imperatival future after (2) shifting the passive construction to active (Matthew).
Only Mark’s account picks up on (a) the Gentile inclusion (perhaps because his
gospel was aimed at a Gentile first readership) and follows (b) the LXX by
using (<i>“all the nations </i>[</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἔθνεσιν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>]<i>”</i>
&lt; “</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>the
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>peoples”
[</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>הָעַמִּים</span><span dir=LTR></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>]). Luke shifts “call” to “be” (c) and <i>CV</i> understands the
final genitive (d) as purposive beyond sheer description. That range of
variants falls within the import of Isaiah’s original wording plus its implications.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Major
Elements of Conversational Style in <i>CV</i></span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>       1.
Extensive use of <u>pro-verbs</u>, that is, auxiliary verbs without their main
verbs: John<b> 16</b>:3 (<i>“So when it happens, remember that I told you it
would”</i>); <b>21</b>:6, 8; Romans <b>14</b>:1; 1 Corinthians <b>8</b>:2; <b>15</b>:16
(<i>“If dead people don’t resurrect, Christ didn’t.”</i>); also Mark <b>11</b>:24,
<i>etc</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>     
 2. Extensive use of <u>contractions</u>, especially (a) with subject pronouns plus
linking verbs: it’s, we’re, we’ve, you’d, you’ll, I’ll (John <b>4</b>:14),
I’ve, we’ll, they’ll, they’re, let’s, he’s (for “he is,” less frequently for
“he has”; cp. Romans <b>10</b>:16, “who’s” for “who has”); Romans <b>8</b>:31 (<u>what</u>’s
for “<i>what </i>does” occurs once.) </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            (b)
with axillary verbs plus negatives: doesn’t, don’t, won’t, can’t, hasn’t,
aren’t, hadn’t, haven’t, isn’t (sparingly; usually substituting pronoun plus
auxiliary: he’s not, <i>etc.</i>)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            (c)
occasionally, a noun plus <u>is</u>: “his work’s not done”: “that God’s unjust”
(Romans <b>3</b>:5); Romans <b>3</b>:13; <i>“We know that no person’s made good
by doing the law” </i>(Galatians <b>2</b>:16).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>       3.
Simplified syntax, choice of current terms; avoidance of slang, regionalisms, cutesy
expressions, words with negative connotations, including lighter words like
“puny,” and shocking language (as might be done with Acts <b>8</b>:20, <i>“To
hell with you and your money”?</i>).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Drawing
a line between current, everyday usage and some level perceived as ignorant and
“uneducated” is somewhat arbitrary and varies from place to place. Vivid
narration is a case in point. Even when the Greek text does use present-tense
verbs in past-tense contexts, <i>CV </i>has switched the verbs into some kind
of past-tense form with superscript </span><sup><span style='font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>p
</span></sup><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>added.
A similar thing can happen occasionally with present-tense indicative verbs
that have future reference (futuristic presents).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Magazine
level on up to technical writing has dispensed with the use of “whom” or “unsplit
infinitives”; so does <i>CV</i>. But our manner of wording here does not weaken
to stylized expressions like “you and I” after prepositions or transitive
verbs—ways of speaking on the street, in mass media sports commentary, or in
dormitory bull sessions. Likewise, it is not obvious that Peter lapsed into
cussing like a foul-mouthed fisherman in the court of the high priest; we are
content to suppose he pronounced curses and took oaths: <i>“taking oaths and
swearing”</i> (Mark <b>14</b>:71); <i>“denied it again with an oath”</i>
(Matthew <b>26</b>:72).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>     
 4. The translation observes <u>current American English punctuation and
spelling rules</u>. They differ somewhat from British custom, from rules at
play in earlier English translations, as well as from the punctuation in edited
Greek texts. <i>The Gregg Reference Manual</i>, 11<sup>th</sup> ed., by William
A. Sabin serves generally  as style guide in such matters.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>    
5. Modern style prefers <u>restrained expression</u>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
1.0in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>That
has led to getting rid of some <u>very</u>’s (elative superlatives) and to
weakening some superlative adjectives and adverbs. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Reducing
the degree of intensive expression has also led to dropping the “verily’s” in
the synoptic gospels as well as the “double verily’s” in John. We have reduced
the perceived overuse of vocatives, leaving them out altogether when the
addressee is obvious from context: John <b>4</b>:15; Acts<b> 15</b>:13; <b>22</b>:6;
2 Thessalonians <b>3</b>:13. Two double vocative remain:  <i>“Saul, Saul”</i>
(Acts <b>9</b>:4; <b>22</b>:7; <b>26</b>:14) and <i>“Jerusalem, Jerusalem” </i>(Matthew
<b>23</b>:37; Luke <b>13</b>:34). That changes <i>“Lord, <s>Lord</s>”</i> (Matthew
<b>7</b>:21, 22; Luke <b>6</b>:46), <i>“Martha, <s>Martha</s>”</i> (Luke <b>10</b>:41),
<i>“Simon, <s>Simon</s>”</i> (Luke <b>22</b>:31), or <i>Rabbi, Rabbi” </i>(Matthew
<b>23</b>:7<sup>ms</sup>). English usage does not usually employ geminated vocatives
except perhaps in certain urgent circumstances: <i>“John! John! don’t harm
yourself!”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Included
under restrained expression is a Hebrew idiom for intensified action: infinitive
absolute plus cognate finite verb. New Testament writers make this expression
into a Greek solecism by a couple unusual constructions: a finite verb + a
cognate dative noun (Matthew <b>13</b>:14; Mark <b>7</b>:10; Acts <b>28</b>:26-27
&lt; Isaiah <b>6</b>:9-10) or a finite verb + cognate participle (Matthew <b>13</b>:14,
right beside the other Greek solecism; Mark <b>4</b>:12; Luke <b>8</b>:10 &lt;
Isaiah <b>6</b>:9). Older English translations created some artificial English constructions
for rendering it: <i>“<u>With desire I have desired</u> to eat this Passover
with you before I suffer”</i> (Luke <b>22</b>:15, ASV). We have used conceptual
equivalency rather than grammatical equivalency, <i>“<u>I have really wanted</u>
to eat this Passover with you before I suffer.”</i> The idiom appears in Acts <b>2</b>:30
(<i>“swear with an oath”</i>); <b>5</b>:4, 28; <b>23</b>:14; as well as in Hebrews
<b>6</b>:14<sup>2</sup> (Greek finite verb + participles) &lt; Genesis <b>22</b>:14).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>5.
After much soul-searching, the <i>CV</i> has replaced <i>“Christ”</i> in the gospels
and Acts with <i>“Messiah”</i> as a translation of </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>χριστός</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
the Greek equivalent of the Hebrew </span><span lang=HE dir=RTL
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>מָשִׁיחַ</span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>, <i>“anointed one.”</i> The idea was to foster a closer
connection between the New Testament term and the Old Testament terminology.
That may help make it clear that Jesus was the fulfillment of the Messianic
hope. In the epistles, <i>Christ</i> takes on the character of a name,
especially in combinations like <i>Jesus Christ</i> and <i>Christ Jesus.
Messiah</i> in such cases takes on the feel of a name besides running counter
to the long-established wording people have become accustomed to seeing. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Attempting
to express the biblical text in conversational style sometimes gets at cross
purposes with preferences in written expression. The frequent use of the
expletives <i>it (is/was)</i> and <i>there (is/are/were)</i> in everyday speaking
is a case in point. Written form aims to eliminate them—along with linking-verb
constructions—in favor of action-verb expressions. When such a conflict arises,
<i>CV</i> has usually chosen the written preferences since the medium here is
writing.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <b>Ambiguities
in the Greek Text<u> </u></b></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>             1.
<u>Capitalization</u> is a judgment call in edited Greek texts, because the earliest
New Testament manuscripts had only capital letters. Distinguishing higher and
lower case presents a few issues.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        a.
Spirit/spirit has some importance because it involves distinguishing the Holy
Spirit from the other seven or so usages of </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πνεῦμα</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
in the New Testament. (See <i>spirit</i> below.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        b.
A few places raise the question whether a word or expression is a description
or a name: Acts <b>14</b>:13 might be <i>“the priest of Zeus/Jupiter Propolis”</i>
or <i>“the priest of Zeus/Jupiter the one being in-front-of the city,”</i> </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὁ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἱερεὺς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τοῦ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Διὸς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τοῦ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὄντος</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πρὸ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τῆς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πόλεως</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.
Philippians <b>4</b>:3 could be <i>“I ask you, true yokefellow, help these
women”</i> or <i>“I ask you, Syzygos/</i></span><i><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Σύζυγος</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
help these women.”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 2 John 1 carries the word </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κυρία</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.
Is it <i>“elect lady”</i> (referencing the church addressed) or <i>“elect
Curia.”</i> Revelation <b>17</b>:5 has <i>“mystery/Mystery”</i> written on the
head of the great harlot. Cp. <i>“The Power of God Called Great”</i> in Acts <b>8</b>:10.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
67.5pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>c.
<u>Lord</u> (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κύριος</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>) covers God’s personal
name Yahveh, a person of superior rank (“lord”), and <i>sir</i> as a general
term of respect (“lord”). New Testament writers never use God’s personal name. They
do not challenge the Jews’ post-exilic sensitivities that went beyond making it
respectful to considering it ineffable. </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Κύριος</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(</span><u><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Lord</span></u><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>in
the Septuagint (a pre-Christian Greek translation of the Old Testament)
replaces the Tetragrammaton (</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>יהוה</span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>).</span><span dir=RTL></span><span dir=RTL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span dir=RTL></span>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>New
Testament quotations and paraphrases of Old Testament passages come from the
Septuagint, instead of being direct translations of the Hebrew. In sum, the
overlapping usages cluster as follows in our pattern: </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <u>Lord</u>
capitalized refers to deity—both for Father and Son.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Lower
case <u>lord</u> applies to persons of honored rank—as an honorific. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <u>Sir</u>
expresses respect aside from rank.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
31.5pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>             <u>Master</u>
addresses a slave owner of superior rank, often translating </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>δεσπότης</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
1.0in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>d.
<u>Son</u> as a reference to Jesus the Messiah is capitalized throughout as a
reference to deity: <i>“You are my Son”</i> (Hebrews <b>1</b>:5 &lt; Psalm <b>2</b>:7).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>             2.
Editors supply <u>textual divisions</u>: paragraphing, chapters, and verses. As
with every translation, we have inserted breaks that make sense to us, most of
them following the Greek or current English-text layouts.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>             3.
The original Greek uncial manuscripts of the New Testament lacked <u>punctuation</u>,
spacing between words, accents, and breathing marks. Those entries in modern Greek
New Testaments come from the editors’ choices. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        a.
The Greek punctuation patterns in the edited New Testament text do not parallel
English usage entirely. Greek has several interrogative words, that is, they
appear in questions so that punctuation is in addition to indicating the
interrogative value of the sentence. In a great number of cases, the text
editors of the Greek New Testament text have decided whether a clause is a <u>statement
or a question</u>. A significant number of passages could have been punctuated either
way: Matthew <b>7</b>:11; <b>8</b>:7; <b>16</b>:3;<b> 17</b>:11; <b>23</b>:37; <b>25</b>:26;
<b>26</b>:45b, 55, 64; <b>27</b>:11, 42; Mark <b>1</b>:24; <b>9</b>:11, 28; <b>14</b>:41,
48, 64; <b>15</b>:2; Luke <b>4</b>:34; <b>7</b>:28; <b>11</b>:13; <b>13</b>:34;
<b>19</b>:22; <b>22</b>:52, 70; <b>23</b>:3; John <b>4</b>:48; <b>6</b>:70; <b>8</b>:25;
<b>12</b>:19; <b>16</b>:19, 31; <b>18</b>:37; <b>20</b>:29; Acts <b>23</b>:9; <b>26</b>:28;
Romans <b>2</b>:27; <b>8</b>:33, 34; <b>11</b>:12, 24; <b>13</b>:3b; 1
Corinthians <b>6</b>:12, 13, 19; <b>11</b>:22;<b> 12</b>:15, 16; <b>15</b>:32;
2 Corinthians <b>10</b>:7; <b>12</b>:19; Hebrews <b>9</b>:14, 17; <b>10</b>:29;
<b>12</b>:7; James<b> 2</b>:1, 18, 19, 22; <b>5</b>:6. Footnotes in Greek
editions alert readers to those possibilities.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        b.
Greek punctuation in the edited texts does not distinguish <u>restrictive and
non-restrictive clauses</u>. Note the difference between these two
punctuations: <i>“Young people need to pay attention to adults, who know what
young people are up against”</i>; <i>vs.</i> <i>“young people need to pay
attention to adults who know what young people are up against.</i>” The first
sentence suggests that adults—at least as a rule—know what young people are up
against. The second sentence directs young people to pay attention to the
adults that <u>do</u> know what young people are up against; of course, some do
not.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        c.
Written Greek lacks <u>exclamation marks</u>; <i>CV</i> has added quite a
number: Mathew <b>7</b>:14; John <b>20</b>:16, 18, 28; <b>21</b>:7, <i>etc.</i>,
altogether about 273. Some texts could be commands or exclamations: 1
Corinthians <b>6</b>:4; Revelation <b>2</b>:7, 11, 17, 29; <b>3</b>:6, 13, 22;
or a statement or exclamation: 1 Corinthians <b>6</b>:5-6; 2 Corinthians <b>10</b>:7;
Galatians<b> 3</b>:1; 1 Thessalonians <b>3</b>:9.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        d.
Should it be a question, statement, or exclamation in John <b>7</b>:28; Acts <b>26</b>:28;
1 Corinthians <b>1</b>:13?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        e.
Q<u>uotation marks</u> do not appear in the Greek text; so it is unclear in some
cases whether the wording is direct or indirect discourse: Matthew <b>2</b>:4,
23; Mark<b> 11</b>:3; Luke <b>19</b>:31; 1 Corinthians<b> 6</b>:12, 13, 18; <b>7</b>:1<sup>2</sup>;
<b>8</b>:1<sup>2</sup>, 4, 6, 8; <b>14</b>:25; 1 John <b>1</b>:6, 8; <b>2</b>:10,
12, 13, 14. Where does the quotation begin in Acts<b> 1</b>:20? </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Other
cues in the context almost always show where a <u>quotation starts</u>, but where
a <u>quotation ends</u> may not be obvious: Matthew <b>21</b>:3; <b>28</b>:7;
Luke <b>7</b>:28; John <b>1</b>:15 or 16 or 18?; <b>3</b>:13 or 15 or 16 or 21?;
<b>3</b>:30 or 36?; Acts<b> 1</b>:17?;<b> 8</b>:26 a or b?; 2 Corinthians <b>2</b>:14
or 21?; Galatians<b> 2</b>:14 or 21?; Colossians<b> 2</b>:21 or 22?; 1 Timothy<b>
4</b>:8a or b or 10?; James <b>2</b>:18 or 19 or 23?. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    As
per standard English practice, quotations within quotations (“embedded
quotations”) have single quotes; quotations within embedded quotations are
double quotes again, and switching back and forth as progressively deeper
embedding occurs: “ ‘ “ ‘ . . .          ’ ” ’ ”.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Double
quotation marks on words near an Old Testament quotation draw attention to
words the New Testament author incorporates into his comments on the quotation.
In Ephesians <b>4</b>:8, <i>e.g</i>., Paul quotes Psalm <b>68</b>:18 and then
lifts the expressions <i>“he ascended” </i>and <i>“gave”</i> out of the Psalm
as he comments on its connection with his point. The practice is quite
widespread in the New Testament, especially in Hebrews, where the writer makes
every major point from an Old Testament text.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Double
quotes also mark words adapted to functional equivalents, suggestive parallels,
or correlative aspects of two pictorial sets: <i>“immerse”</i> you in the Holy
Spirit (Mark<b> 1</b>:8<sup>2</sup>; Luke <b>3</b>:16; John <b>1</b>:26, 31, 33<sup>2</sup>;
Acts <b>1</b>:5; <b>11</b>:16<sup>2</sup>); <i>“law”</i> of Christ (Galatians <b>6</b>:2;
James <b>1</b>:25); <i>“circumcision”</i> (Colossians <b>2</b>:11), <i>“dead,”</i>
and <i>“made you alive”</i> in Colossians <b>2</b>:11-13; <i>“father”</i>
(Romans <b>4</b>:16); <i>“wisdom”</i> (James <b>3</b>:15).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                 f.
The placement of a <u>comma</u> can alter the sense of a passage: John <b>1</b>:3-4,
<i>“Without him not even one thing came to be that came to be. </i><b><sup>4</sup></b><i>In
him was life.”</i> or <i>“Without him not even one thing came to be. What came
to be <sup>4</sup>in him was life”</i> [Westcott &amp; Hort NT text]. CV ends
up, however, rewording the flow of thought: <i>“</i></span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>nothing
came into existence without him.”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> A similar uncertainty
appears in Ephesians <b>1</b>:4; should <i>“in love”</i> connect with the next
verse or does it modify some previous entry like <i>“holy and blameless”</i> or
perhaps <i>“chose”</i> (that is, <i>“lovingly chose”</i>)? Such possibilities
arise elsewhere as per Paul’s habit of attaching a short prepositional phrase
after an otherwise full statement.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                 g.
We have supplied <u>underlining</u> in some places to give clarity, show
emphasis, or mark contrast. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Some
of our underlining comes from indications in the Greek text itself; those include
passages that show (1) nominative-case pronouns. Since Greek verbs have
person-and-number endings, a subject pronoun is unnecessary. When the Greek
text does use a nominative-case pronoun, the usual inference is that it shows <u>emphasis</u>
or marks <u>contrast</u>. In many of those cases that call for emphasis, we
have underlined the English pronoun subject. English almost always states the
pronoun subject; so we add underlining to indicate the emphasis. Spoken English
does the emphasizing by raising pitch and volume slightly and perhaps adding a
short pause afterwards. Such cases include Romans <b>11</b>:22, 23, 24; 1
Corinthians <b>5</b>:9 + 11;<b> 6</b>:8; <b>9</b>:25; 2 Corinthians <b>4</b>:13;
<b>13</b>:7; Galatians <b>1</b>:8; <b>6</b>:1; 1 Thessalonians <b>5</b>:4; 2
Timothy<b> 3</b>:10; Hebrews <b>12</b>:25; James <b>2</b>:18. John’s gospel
uses subject pronouns so often that emphasis does not seem to have been his
intention. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Many
other texts with Greek subject pronouns do not have English underlining because
the subject pronoun marks contrast more than emphasis; English does not necessarily
stress the pronoun in such cases: <i>“the dead will resurrect incorruptible,
and we’ll be changed”</i> (1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:52). <i>“We”</i> and <i>“the
dead”</i> do not so much indicate contrast as equal entries in a list of
alternatives. In sum, if the underlining is a subject pronoun, it is because
the Greek states a pronoun subject in an apparently emphatic entry; other underlined
words represent the translator’s choice to clarify written communication in
comparison to oral presentation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Aside
from pronoun subjects, (2) underlining draws attention to lists or </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (3)
aids in proper reading for <u>clarity</u>. In Luke <b>15</b>:29-30, underlining
draws attention to the prodigal’s <i>“grain-fed calf”</i> <i>vs.</i> the older
brother’s <i>“kid.” </i>Underlining for clarity draws attention to a list
spread across a context: <i>“donate, pray, fast”</i> (Matthew <b>6</b>:2-10); <i>“people
passing by, chief priests, rebels” </i>(Matthew <b>27</b>:39-44); <i>“good
shepherd/owner, hireling, wolf”</i> (John <b>9</b>:10-16). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (4)
Underlining for <u>emphasis</u> (aside from pronoun subjects) corrects a
possible misreading of the text: <i>“Nobody can come to me unless the <u>Father</u>
that sent me is drawing him”</i> rather than <i>“Nobody can come to me unless
the Father that sent me <u>draws</u> him”</i> (John <b>6</b>:44). The contrast
is with other <u>people’s</u> interests drawing him (Jewish national
independence <i>vs.</i> Jesus’ spiritual kingdom—John <b>18</b>:36). Other
places where our translation has supplied emphasis include Romans <b>2</b>:23;<b>
3</b>:20-23; <b>4</b>:11-16; <b>5</b>:3-4, 7, 17, 20; <b>7</b>:18; <b>8</b>:17;
<b>9</b>:32-33; <b>10</b>:5; 14:14; 1 Corinthians <b>2</b>:10; <b>4</b>:8; <b>14</b>:15;
<b>15</b>:13; Philippians <b>2</b>:27; <b>3</b>:4; 1 Thessalonians <b>4</b>:10-12;
Hebrews <b>9</b>:13-14; 1 John <b>2</b>:24. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                 h.
<u>Italics</u> identify added words not in the original. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (a)
That happens when Greek uses the article rather than the possessive modifier (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>αὐτοῦ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>/his,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἐμοῦ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>/my,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>σοῦ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>/your,
<i>etc.</i>). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (b)
It happens when Greek uses elliptical expressions without repeating some of the
words common to both parts: repeated verbs, direct objects, indirect objects,
prepositional phrases in the next segment; Acts<b> 10</b>:23, <i>“having called
them in, he lodged</i> them<i>”</i>; 1 Corinthians<b> 11</b>:9, <i>“Man was not
created on account of woman, but woman </i>was created<i> on account of man.”</i>
In such circumstances, English often wants to restate those words. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (c)
Greek may omit forms of the verb <u>to be</u> even when not in repeat segments
if they are timeless/equative/linking/copulative usages (1 Corinthians <b>8</b>:6,
<i>“To us </i>there is<i> one God”</i>) rather than time words (<i>“He </i>is <i>in
the inner rooms” </i>[Matthew <b>24</b>:26]); 1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:39, <i>“Not
all flesh </i>is <i>the same kind of flesh.” </i>(unitalicized here in
italicized quotation)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (d)
Italics go into the text when <i>CV</i> adds in phrases to clarify its meaning
in the way the translator understands it. Romans <b>3</b>:31 says, “<i>So are
we contradicting law </i>by advocating <i>trust? Goodness, no! Instead, </i>we’re
establishing <i>the purpose of </i>law <i>another way</i>. Romans <b>12</b>:7
reads, <i>“</i>If <i>it’s </i>service<i>, let’s carry out our commitment to him
</i>in <i>our </i>service<i>.”</i> Such additions can shape the meaning
significantly. That is the reason for showing by italics what has been added
beyond the author’s own words.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (e)
Italics also mark all direct quotations from speakers in the text, from the Old
Testament, or other sources. (Unitalicized words within italicized quotes
identify added words within direct quotations.) </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  i.
<u>Bold type</u>—along with italics—indicates Old Testament
quotations—accompanied at the end by small bold-letter scripture references in
brackets. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  j.
<u>Parentheses</u> within the text provide the original author’s explanatory
comments identified by editors of the Greek text: Matthew<b> 21</b>:26; <b>27</b>:18,
33, 52-53; Mark <b>5</b>:8; <b>7</b>:3-4; <b>5</b>:10; Luke <b>23</b>:19; <b>24</b>:10;
John <b>2</b>:21-22; <b>3</b>:24; <b>4</b>:2, 8; <b>6</b>:1, 6, 71; <b>7</b>:5,
39, [[<b>8</b>:6]], <b>8</b>:22, 27-28; <b>9</b>:22; <b>11</b>:6; <b>12</b>:16,
33; <b>13</b>:11; <b>14</b>:22; <b>18</b>:9, 32, 40; <b>19</b>:17, 31, 35; <b>21</b>:7,
19; Acts <b>1</b>:15; <b>5</b>:1; <b>9</b>:36; <b>12</b>:3; <b>13</b>:8; <b>17</b>:18,
21; <b>25</b>:3; Romans <b>3</b>:5, 8, 19, 22; 1 Corinthians <b>1</b>:16; <b>7</b>:11,
21; <b>14</b>:20; <b>15</b>:6; 2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:7; <b>6</b>:13; <b>9</b>:4;
<b>13</b>:13; Galatians <b>2</b>:6; Ephesians <b>2</b>:5; <b>5</b>:9; <b>6</b>:3;
Philippians <b>1</b>:23, 24; Colossians <b>4</b>:10; 1 Thessalonians <b>4</b>:5;
1 Timothy <b>3</b>:5; Philemon 19; Hebrews <b>6</b>:18; <b>7</b>:11;<b> 11</b>:4;
<b>13</b>:2, 17; 1 Peter <b>5</b>:2; Revelation <b>2</b>:9; <b>9</b>:16; <b>14</b>:18;
<b>20</b>:5 (about 80 instances).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  k.
<u>Brackets</u> within quoted material are the original author’s explanatory
comments. In a few cases, we have added our own italicized brackets of
explanations within the text: Matthew <b>2</b>:23; Luke <b>9</b>:7; John <b>10</b>:22;
Acts <b>27</b>:9; Galatians <b>2</b>:12; Revelation <b>6</b>:15; <b>9</b>:7, 9,
11;<b> 16</b>:16;<b> 19</b>:18; <b>21</b>:20<sup>2</sup>. [ x x ]<sup>ms</sup>
indicates textually questionable wording.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            </span><span
dir=RTL></span><span dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span dir=RTL></span> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>     l.
Word division does not create a problem in the New Testament except in 1
Corinthians <b>15</b>:32, where Paul invokes the Aramaic expression </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Μαρανα</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>θα</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
[</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>מרנא תא</span><span dir=LTR></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>]</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>, <i>“Lord, come!”</i> The less likely
division could be </span><i><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Μ</span></i><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>αραν</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>αθα</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
[</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>מרנ אתא</span><span dir=LTR></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>], <i>“Our Lord has come!”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ambiguities
in the Greek Language Itself</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *1.
Avoiding <u>participle constructions</u>, particularly introductory and
mid-sentence participial phrases. Introductory adverbial clauses can replace
them in English: Mark <b>1</b>:16, <i>e.g.</i> Sometimes the two expressions
can become two independent clauses. Greek loves participles; so changing them
to finite verbs and other substitutes happens quite often in <i>CV</i>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Replacing
participles goes hand in hand with avoiding the <u>hypotactic syntax</u>
created by participles—creating verbal elements that have a different
grammatical level from the main clause. Hypotactic arrangement calls for
attaching dependent clauses and verbal phrases onto independent clauses (ones
that make complete sense standing alone). It does so by participles,
subordinate conjunctions plus clauses, gerund phrases, infinitive phrases,
adverbial clauses, and relative clauses. In many such cases, English prefers to
reduce the number of <u>paratactic constructions</u>, that is, segments of
equal grammatical levels (unless adverbial, relative, purposes, or nominal
clauses create the subordination). Making the switch from hypotactic to
paratactic syntax in conversational translation has been a formidable task!
Note also #41 below.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        The
abundant use of participles in the Greek text creates a recurrent source of
ambiguity. Participles can “cover for” all aspects and concepts associated with
verbs: time, means, manner, cause, condition, concession, attendant
circumstance, purpose, result—even command (rarely). Readers must judge from
the topic and context which possibilities apply—if any one of them specially
applies; translators face that same task. Their renderings are judgment calls
that give a more specific meaning than the Greek text itself expresses. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        The
most accurate way to translate a Greek participle is to use an English
participle. Participles by their nature—in any language—have the range of
possibilities listed in our previous paragraph. Using a participle in the
receptor language keeps ambiguity at the same level as in the original
language. Getting rid of participles to satisfy English custom of usage forces
a choice, and that presses translation somewhat into interpretation. Thankfully,
nothing significant is hardly ever at stake. If that were not so, the New
Testament writers themselves would have created uncertainty for their own readers
by the prolific use of participles.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *2.
The second main source of ambiguity in New Testament Greek comes from the
abundant use of the <u>genitive-case nouns</u> tied to other nouns. The
writers’ Hebrew-speaking background probably fostered adopting such wording
more frequently. Hebrew syntax habitually puts nouns in <u>“construct”
relationship</u> with other nouns to show <u>that</u> they are connected. Hearers—by
their own perception based on experience—supply <u>how</u> they are connected. <i><u>Of</u></i><u>
expressions</u> in English replicate pretty much that same range of ambiguity,
but in many instances, they are not satisfying to the English ear. The most
frequent use of the Greek genitive case is for tying one noun to another
grammatically. That does not indicate <u>how</u> the referents of the two nouns
are connected really or conceptually. Eliminating “of constructions” means
choosing between appositive genitive, descriptive genitive, genitive of cause,
genitive of material, genitive of source; genitive of value, objective genitive
<i>vs.</i> subjective genitive, (partitive genitive), possessive genitive, and
some relationships that are difficult to classify because they have merely
topical connection. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Not
every possible genitive value can apply to every usage, but the choice between
just two can be challenging. Romans <b>8</b>:35 affords an example of the
dilemma between subjective and objective genitive. The issue comes up when a
genitive noun for a person follows a noun for an action: <i>“What will separate
us from the love of Christ?”</i> Does Paul mean the love Christ has for us
(Christ’s love) or the love we have for Christ (our loving Christ)? By going
ahead to<b> 8</b>:37, we get an answer: <i>“the one who loved us.”</i> So <i>“love
of Christ”</i> means <i>“Christ’s love for us” </i>(subjective genitive); hence,
the sufferings Paul lists do not indicate that Christ does not love us. Romans <b>11</b>:15
is another instance of needing to decide between objective and subjective
genitive. The </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πϊστις</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
