lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>χριστοῦ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(<i>“faith of Christ”</i>) expression affords another famous example: Romans <b>3</b>:22,
26; Galatians <b>2</b>:16<sup>2</sup>, 20; <b>3</b>:22; Philippians<b> 3</b>:9,
and Ephesians <b>3</b>:12. Is it <i>“faithfulness of Christ”</i> or <i>“(our) trust
in Christ”</i>?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        In
some cases, replacing <i>of </i>pushes a translator into circumlocution, and
that can break up the flow of thought. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <u>Partitive
genitives</u> after verbs indicate that the action affects “part of” the
subsequent noun instead the whole direct object. English speakers automatically
understand that nobody drinks <i>“(all) the milk from the flock”</i> (1
Corinthians<b> 9</b>:7) as surely as Adam and Eve did were not to eat all the
fruit of the tree of knowledge (<i>eat of</i> is the Hebraism here for source
or part; Genesis <b>2</b>:16-17).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <u>Descriptive
genitive</u> is the most foreign to English idiom: <i>“sons of disobedience” </i>becomes
<i>“disobedient people” </i>(Colossians <b>3</b>:6); <i>“the bodies of our
humiliation”</i> means <i>“our humble bodies”</i> (Philippians <b>3</b>:21), <i>“by
the word of the power of him”</i> becomes <i>“his powerful word”</i> (Hebrews<b>
1</b>:3). We have opted in Ephesians<b> 4</b>:3 to say <i>“spiritual unity”</i>
rather than <i>“unity of the s/Spirit.” </i>The passage raises two issues at
once: capitalization of Spirit/spirit and the force of the “of expression”
(genitive case). Is it a spiritual unity (<i>vs.</i> a physical one), or is it
a unity overseen/caused in some way by the Holy Spirit?  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        A
similar import—descriptive genitive—is conveyed by bringing “sons of” into the
wording: <i>“You are <u>sons of</u> the ones that murdered the prophets”</i>
(Matthew <b>23</b>:31). The phraseology evidently does not mean <i>“You are <u>descendants
of</u> the murderers,” </i>but <i>“you’re <u>like</u> the ones that murdered
the prophets.”</i> (Otherwise, Jesus’ argument would seem questionable.) In all
these cases, the second noun refers to a characteristic that English <i>of</i>
ties to the first noun; English prefers to put that characteristic in an
adjective form that modifies that first noun. As said before, the probable
reason descriptive genitives appear more often in the New Testament is that the
writers were Jewish. Hebrew had few adjectives and made up for it by using a
two-noun construction comprised of a “construct noun” tied to a subsequent absolute
noun. That arrangement got reproduced in Greek by a noun plus genitive noun,
and that in turn comes into English literally in a <i>“____ of ____”</i>
format.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        We
have tried to get rid of “<u>genitive strings</u>” (catenations): <i>“to the
praise of the glory of the grace of him” </i>(Ephesians <b>1</b>:6); <i>“the
light of the gospel of the glory of Jesus Christ”</i> (2 Corinthians <b>4</b>:4);
<i>“the light of the knowledge of the glory of God”</i> (2 Corinthians<b> 4</b>:6).
<i>“The riches of the glory of this mystery” becomes “the value of this
glorious mystery”</i> (Colossians <b>1</b>:27).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            3.
The second person plural always has the same form in the Greek indicative and
imperative moods in the present tense. In places, more than one translation fits:
<i>“Are you sleeping?”</i> or <i>“Sleep on!”</i> (Mark <b>14</b>:41, in
Gethsemane); Matthew <b>26</b>:25; John <b>14</b>:11; 1 Peter <b>1</b>:6 (<i>“rejoice”
</i>or <i>“you rejoice”</i>).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:4.5pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:31.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>4.
In several cases, </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὅτι</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> (<i>that</i>) could
introduce indirect discourse or an adverbial clause (<i>because</i>): Matthew <b>8</b>:16;
<b>12</b>:32; <b>23</b>:31; Luke <b>16</b>:8; <b>19</b>:34; John <b>4</b>:27; <b>14</b>:2,
28; <b>20</b>:13; Acts <b>6</b>:1; <b>24</b>:11; Philippians <b>2</b>:16; Hebrews
<b>12</b>:7; 1 John <b>2</b>:14; Revelation <b>21</b>:5.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        5.
The genitive singular masculine and neuter of second declension is the same form
(</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>ου</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>). That creates something of an
uncertainty in whether a pronoun like </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>αὐτοῦ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
means <i>“of it”</i> (for instance <i>“of [the paschal lamb]”</i>) or <i>“of
him”</i> (the functional equivalent person of the paschal lamb (John <b>19</b>:36).
            </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
49.5pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
problem comes up familiarly in the model prayer: <i>from evil</i> or <i>from</i>
<i>the evil one</i> (Matthew<b> 6</b>:13). Other instances are Matthew <b>5</b>:37;
<b>13</b>:19; 38; Luke <b>11</b>:4<sup>ms</sup>; John <b>17</b>:15; Ephesians <b>6</b>:16;
2 Thessalonians <b>3</b>:3; 1 John <b>2</b>:13-14; <b>3</b>:12; <b>5</b>:18,
19. Invoking the principle of interpreting the unclear in light of the clear,<i>
CV</i> has used 2 John<b> 2</b>:13 as the clear case and then applied that
understanding to some of the other cases. The first usage (<i>from evil</i>)
appears in Luke <b>6</b>:45; Acts <b>28</b>:21; Romans <b>12</b>:9; 1
Thessalonians <b>5</b>:22.  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
49.5pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Did
Jesus tell the disciples to drink of the cup or of the wine at the Last Supper
(Matthew <b>26</b>:27 = Mark <b>14</b>:23)?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        6.
The indefinite pronoun </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τις</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>/</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τι</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>or
interrogative pronoun </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τίς</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>/</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τί</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>can
mean <i>what</i> or <i>who</i> even in the masculine-feminine form </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τίς</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.
Romans<b> 8</b>:35 asks, </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τίς</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἡμᾶς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>χωρίσει</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἀπὸ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τῆς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἀγάπης</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τοῦ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Χριστός</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>;
<i>“What/who will separate us from the love of Christ?”</i> The problem comes
from the fact that Greek has grammatical gender; English has only sex
reference. In Greek, nouns for male and female <u>sex</u> references are (almost)
always masculine and feminine <u>grammatical gender</u>. But neuter does not
match asexual things; neuter things (sexually speaking) can be masculine
feminine, or neuter (grammatically speaking). In this passage, the choice needs
to be <i>“what”</i> because the apostle accompanies his question with another
question that lists (asexual) difficulties: </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>θλίψις</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἢ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>στενοχωρία</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἢ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>διωγμὸς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἢ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>λιμὸς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἢ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>γυμνότης</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἢ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κίνδυνος</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἢ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>μάχαιρα</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(“<i>Will
tribulation or distress or persecution or famine or nakedness or danger or
sword separate us from his loving us?”</i>) All the words in that list are
grammatically masculine or feminine; so the reference in </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τίς</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(grammatically mas or fem) is to these grammatically m/f items in the context,
not to people, who are not part of the discussion. In English those list items
are neuter and so call for <i>what</i>, not <i>who</i>, despite the
masculine/feminine form of the Greek pronoun at the start of the verse.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        7.
Definite <i>vs.</i> indefinite nouns in Hebrew construct form bring up an
ambiguity. When that construction comes into Greek by quotation or thought
pattern, the problem comes with it. A Hebrew noun in construct tied to a
subsequent noun cannot carry the Hebrew definite article. So, in the case of </span><span
lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>מלאך</span><span
lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>‾יהוה</span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span> the translation could be <i><u>an</u> angel of the Lord</i> or <i><u>the</u>
angel of the Lord</i>. The typical translation in, say, Genesis<b> 22</b>:11
says <i><u>the</u> angel of the Lord</i>, as if there is one angel involved in
this kind of activity or that has rank above others. English translations of
Genesis<b> 22</b>:11 usually say <i>the angel of the Lord. </i>The LXX
(Septuagint Greek translation of the Old Testament) says, </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄγγελος</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κυρίου</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
which would normally become <i><u>an</u> angel of the Lord</i> in English<i>.</i>
The Jewish translators of the LXX evidently had some reason for making <i>angel</i>
anarthrous here. The LXX here does not follow the rule that if the second noun
is definite the construct noun is definite as well—or at least as definite as
the phrase head. Perhaps those translators conceived of the full expression as
a title. At any rate, we are left with an English conundrum: what to do with
the parallel cases in the New Testament. The English article on the Hebrew noun
in construct appears if the second Hebrew noun is definite, a situation that
could include God’s personal name as something definite. But this Genesis text
does not evidence that take on the construction. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    We
opted to use <i><u>an</u> angel of the Lord</i> for </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄγγελος</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κυρίου</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>and
change the expression to <i>the angel</i> when articular. The angel could be
Gabriel in all the references associated with Messiah’s birth as he was in the
Annunciation to Mary (Luke <b>1</b>:26). In the angelic appearance to the
shepherds, Luke’s account begins in<b> 2</b>:9 with <i>an angel</i> and
switches to <i>the angel</i> in<b> </b>9, 10, 13. The pattern reflects the use
of the Greek article on nouns that reappear after being used without the
article the first time (anaphoric nouns). English follows the same practice.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Ambiguities
in English Relative to Greek</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            </span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Greek
has some precision that requires special handling to reproduce in English.
Greek has personal endings on its finite verbs; so subject pronouns are not
needed. But Greek does have nominative-case pronouns. If they do appear with
the finite verbs, they imply emphasis—that is, contrast usually. That nuance in
spoken English is covered by a special pitch pattern with perhaps a pause
following. In written form, underlining can cover it, as in Matthew <b>28</b>:5<i>:
“<u>You</u> don’t need to be afraid”</i> in contrast to the guards at Jesus’
tomb, who were terrified (?) Underlined subject pronouns often represent the
presence of subject pronouns in the original text.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        The
verb endings and pronouns for second person are different for singular and
plural in Greek whereas English uses <i>you/your</i> for both numbers. A
superscript <sup>sg </sup>or <sup>pl</sup> appears in the English <i>CV</i> wording
to clarify the reference if the context is ambiguous.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-.5in'><span style='font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Occasionally,
<sup>mas </sup>or</span><sup><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> fem</span></sup><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> appears after an
English word to show that the Greek has a masculine or feminine reference: 1
Timothy <b>5</b>:16, <i>“If a believer<sup>fem</sup> has widows . . .”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Changes
from Greek/Hebrew to English Custom of Usage</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            English
shares many features with Greek and Hebrew, especially “standard English,” which
exhibits a more “trained usage.” Standard English adheres to traditional
“rules” of grammar, with a healthy amount of participle constructions, gerunds,
upper-case pronouns, long compound and complex sentences. (a) The differences in
going from Greek to English combine with additional differences going (b) from
earlier English to current usage and then (c) from current “standard English”
to current conversational style (part III). The entries below look at shifts
from Greek to English. Subsequent entries address current and conversational
matters.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *1.
<u>Nuanced Greek verbs</u>. More frequently than other versions, <i>CV</i> has nuanced
Greek present and imperfect verbs with wording for conative, ingressive/inceptive,
and progressive action. It has even used a few progressive futures: Luke <b>17</b>:35;
John <b>8</b>:12; <b>16</b>:23; Hebrews <b>8</b>:11. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Using
the imperfect <u>progressive action</u> seems to make better sense in Luke<b> 4</b>:41,
for example: <i>“He wouldn’t let demons <u>keep</u> talking when they declared
he was God’s Son.”</i> They had already actually said he was God’s Son; so the
verse does not seem to mean he kept them from saying it at all. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        In
the other direction, we have significantly reduced Greek’s (and Hebrew’s)
“obsession” with indicating the beginning of an action (ingressive action<i>)—“began
to, arise and __”</i>: Matthew <b>26</b>:22; Mark <b>15</b>:18; Luke <b>13</b>:25;
John <b>14</b>:31; Acts <b>1</b>:1, <i>etc.</i> Classical Greek more often covered
<u>inceptive/ingressive action</u> with the aorist tense; but in the process of
creating this translation, we have come think that aspect in Hellenistic New
Testament Greek comes quite often with the imperfect tense—as in Latin. What seem
to be aorist and imperfect ingressives both appear in Matthew <b>2</b>:9, <i>“</i></span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>They
started traveling, and the star they saw/had-seen when they were in the East
started going ahead of them till it stood above where the child was.”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Comparatively
speaking, the New Testament highlights ingressive action more frequently than
English does. Acts <b>1</b>:1 is a rather familiar example, where Luke adds </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄρχω</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(“begin”) to the verbs. Commentaries frequently indicate that the <i>“<u>began</u>
to do and to teach”</i> adds little to the meaning. Well, it adds little to the
meaning from the standpoint of English idiom, but it is not a pointless word.
English custom of usage is just not as inclined to call attention to the
inception of an act. Note similarly Luke <b>23</b>:30, <i>“They will begin to
say to the mountains . . .” </i>instead of <i>“they will say to the mountains.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        The
<u>conative value</u> of the Greek present tense appears in our wording of
Paul’s comments in 1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:32-34 about differing concerns of
married and unmarried men and women. <i>CV</i> uses <i>“feel a need” </i>to be
pleasing to spouses.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Behind
these comments about verbal nuances lies the fact that English has an even more
replete verb system than New Testament Greek did, especially for expressing verbal
aspects. The same present-tense verb form in Greek could translate as <i>“they
stone,” “they are stoning,” “they are trying to stone,” “they keep stoning,”
“they <u>do</u> stone</i> (people there, emphatic).<i>”</i> (There is also the
historic present that we have avoided—a present-tense form referring to a past action.)
Note as well the futuristic present (1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:32, <i>etc</i>.). The
same imperfect-tense form could translate as <i>“they were stoning</i> (simple
linear),<i>”</i> <i>“they <u>were too</u> stoning (him; </i>emphatic linear<i>),”</i>
<i>“they <u>tried to</u> stone</i> (conative),<i>”</i> <i>“they <u>kept</u>
stoning,”</i> <i>“they <u>started</u> stoning</i> (ingressive),<i>”</i> <i>“they
<u>would</u> stone </i>(people whenever execution was in order),<i>”</i> <i>“they
<u>used to</u> stone</i> (people in ancient Israel—but probably do so no longer).<i>”</i>
It could also translate into English as <i>“they stoned” </i>(simple past) if
context already makes clear the “linear character” of the author’s interest in
the action. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *2.
<u>Negating the verb rather than the direct object</u>: <i>“I have no silver or
gold” </i>(Acts <b>3</b>:6) becomes <i>“I don’t have any silver or gold.” “They
don’t have any wine”</i> replaces <i>“They have no wine”</i> (John <b>2</b>:2);
cp. John <b>7</b>:26.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            3.
<u>Negatives preceding what they negate</u>: <i>“Forbid him not” </i>becomes <i>“Don’t
forbid him”</i> (Mark<b> 9</b>:39). English translations from the 1940’s on
have discarded the older sequence.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            4.
<u>Relative negative</u>. We have sometimes translated as relative negatives
what in form of expression are direct opposites. Readers can tell from the kind
of action that the two elements are not exclusive opposites, but are different
degrees of the same thing or function together rather than separately.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (1)
<i>and not</i> translated <i>not so much</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Matthew<b>
9</b>:13: <i>“I didn’t come to call righteous, but sinners” </i>becomes</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                            <i> “I
didn’t come so much to call good people, as to call </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                            sinners”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>;
cp. Mark <b>2</b>:17; Luke <b>5</b>:32</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Matthew
<b>9</b>:13 &amp; <b>12</b>:7 &lt; Hosea <b>6</b>:6</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                             <i>“I
want mercy and not sacrifice”</i> becomes </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                            <i> “I
want mercy more than sacrifice.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    John<b>
6</b>:26: <i>“not because you saw signs, but because you ate the loaves”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                becomes</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                “</span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>not
so much because you saw signs, but because you ate bread”</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John<b>
12</b>:30: <i>“That voice didn’t come for my sake but for yours”</i> becomes</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                 <i>“That
voice didn’t come for my sake so much as for yours.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(1
Corinthians <b>10</b>:24):<i> “Don’t look after your own welfare as much as </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                other
people’s welfare.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1
Corinthians <b>14</b>:22<sup>2</sup>: </span><b><i><sup><span style='font-size:
14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“</span></sup></i></b><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>So
</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>other
</span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>languages
serve as signs for unbelievers, </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                            </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>more
than</span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
believers; prophecy is for believers, </span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>more than</span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                            unbelievers.”</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                              </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1
Corinthians <b>15</b>:10: <i>“Not I, but God’s grace in me”</i> becomes </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                <i>“not
</i>so much<i> I, but God’s grace in me”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2
Corinthians<b> 5</b>:7: <i>“We walk by faith, not by sight”</i> becomes</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                             <i>“We
operate by faith more than by sight.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Philippians<b>
2</b>:4: <i>“</i></span><b><sup><span style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>4</span></sup></b><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Don’t
just look at your own interests, but at the </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                interests
of other people too.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                              </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1
Timothy</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'> 1</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:9: <i>“</i></span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>[The
law is] not there </span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>so much<i> for good people, as for </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;text-align:justify'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                          lawless,
disorderly . . . ”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1
John<b> 4</b>:10: “<i>not that we loved God, but that he loved us</i>” becomes</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                 </span><b><sup><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“</span></sup></b><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>not
so much that we’ve loved God as he’s loved us.</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>”
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (2)
verb opposites</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Romans<b>
9</b>:3: </span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>“I loved Jacob and hated Esau”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
becomes</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                                        
</span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“I
loved Jacob more than Esau.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Note
also 1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:22; <b>15</b>:10; 1 Timothy <b>3</b>:9; James <b>2</b>:24.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            5.
<u>Eliminating pendens constructions</u>: <i>“Those parts of the body that we
think are less honorable—on them we bestow more abundant honor”</i> (1
Corinthians <b>12</b>:23). Getting rid of the pendens makes the sentence, <i>“Parts
we consider less honorable we give more attention to.”</i> 1 Corinthians<b> 16</b>:3,
<i>“Whoever you approve—through letters I will send them to deliver your gift
to Jerusalem.”</i> Cp. Matthew <b>5</b>:19; Mark <b>6</b>:16; John<b> 3</b>:26;<b>
6</b>:39; <b>7</b>:20; 1 Corinthians <b>16</b>:3; Galatians <b>3</b>:7;
Revelation <b>2</b>:24; <b>17</b>:16. Sometimes pendens constructions are hard
to eliminate: note 1 John <b>1</b>:1-3. <i>CV</i> retains a double-subject
pendens in Romans <b>2</b>:22: <i>“You that detest idols, do you rob temples?”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            6.
<u>Gerunds preferred over infinitives</u> in noun slots. Infinitives in such
places carry a quaint feel in English. Greek infinitives in noun slots come
into English best as gerunds: <i>“They kept the matter to themselves and
wondered what ‘<u>arising</u> from the dead’ meant”</i> <i>vs.</i> <i>“wondered
what ‘<u>to rise</u> from the dead meant’” </i>(Mark <b>9</b>:10).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            7.
<u>Eliminating historical presents</u> in the historical books: especially
frequent in Mark’s gospel: <b>1</b>:12, 21; <b>8</b>:6, 17, 22; <b>9</b>:2, <i>etc.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            8.
<u>Eliminating futuristic presents</u>: 1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:32, <i>“We’re
going to die tomorrow.” In place of “Tomorrow we die</i> [</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἀποθνῄσκομεν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>].”
<i>“I’ll come to you when I pass through Macedonia,” for “I pass through
Macedonia”</i> (1 Corinthians <b>16</b>:5). Note also Matthew <b>26</b>:18; Mark
<b>4</b>:38; <b>14</b>:41; Luke <b>13</b>:32; John <b>12</b>:34, 48.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            9.
<u>Eliminating imperatival futures</u>. The construction is something of a
Hebraism that increased in New Testament Greek usage via influence from the
Septuagint. Everyday English rarely says, <i>“You shall not pass.”</i> If it
does, it carries strong emphasis—as in the example. <i>“You will stay home this
evening, Johnny” </i>gets restated with the future command form <i>“going to
__”</i>: <i>“You’re going to stay home this evening.”</i> Cp. Matthew<b> 23</b>:24.
We rendered as command forms what are apparently imperatival futures.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            10.
<u>Linking verbs</u>. Action verbs replace linking verbs whenever possible. Sometimes
we have “covered them up” inside of contractions with the subject.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            11.
<u>Timeless aorist (gnomic/omnitemporal)</u>. We rendered as normal English
presents (or perfects) what seem like <u>timeless aorists</u> in the Greek: Matthew
<b>3</b>:17; <b>11</b>:19; 13:44?, 46?, 48?, <b>17</b>:5; <b>18</b>:23; <b>23</b>:2;
Mark <b>1</b>:11; Luke<b> 3:22; 7</b>:35; <b>11</b>:52?, <b>12</b>:3?; John <b>15</b>:6,
8?;<b> 17</b>:25<sup>3</sup>;<b> </b>Romans <b>3</b>:23 (have sinned); <b>8</b>:24
(have been saved); Galatians <b>5</b>:24 (have crucified); James <b>1</b>:11; 1
Peter <b>1</b>:24; 1 John<b> 4</b>:8. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *12.
<u>Active transforms</u>. Passive-verb sentences can change into active ones
whenever the actor in the verb is known: John <b>1</b>:2, 10; 1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:54,
and many other texts. <i>CV</i> has changed passive to active in Galatians <b>5</b>:11
even though the agent is not specified in the wording.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>13.
<u>Reduced number of vocatives</u>. Sometimes they move to the front of the
sentence: 1 Corinthians <b>4</b>:6, 10; <b>15</b>:1. In other cases they fall
out when they seem unnecessarily frequent: Matthew <b>14</b>:28, 29; <b>18</b>:21;
<b>25</b>:22, 37, 44; <b>26</b>:22; Acts <b>7</b>:25. See below under <i>Lord</i>,
<i>Master</i>, <i>brother</i>, and the aging word <i>beloved</i>, which appears
often in the vocative: Romans 7:4; 1 Corinthians <b>10</b>:14; <b>15</b>:58; 2
Corinthians <b>7</b>:1;<b> 12</b>:19; Philippians <b>2</b>:12; Philemon 1;
Hebrews <b>6</b>:9; James <b>1</b>:16, 19; <b>2</b>:5; 1 Peter <b>2</b>:11; <b>4</b>:12;
2 Peter <b>3</b>:1, 15; 1 John <b>3</b>:2, 21; <b>4</b>:7, 11; 2 John 5, 11;
Jude 20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            14.
<u>Measurements</u> of time, volume, and distance. These come into English in
modern units because they stay the same over time. <i>CV </i>leaves<i> </i>monetary
values in the original Jewish, Greek, and Roman coinage because inflation keeps
adjusting the equivalency.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            15.
<u>Word order</u>. <i>CV</i> has changed some <u>word orders</u> to fit English
custom: <i>“earth and sky”</i> rather than <i>“heaven and earth”</i>
(Revelation <b>21</b>:1); <i>“day and night”</i> <i>vs.</i> <i>“night and day”</i>
(2 Timothy <b>1</b>:3); <i>“you and I”</i> <i>vs.</i> the text’s sequence (Luke
<b>8</b>:17; Acts <b>15</b>:9; 1 Corinthians<b> 4</b>:6, 10, 12-15; <b>9</b>:6;
2 Corinthians <b>1</b>:19); <i>“feet and hands”</i> becomes <i>“hands and feet”</i>
(Acts <b>21</b>:11); <i>“fall and rise”</i> becomes <i>“rise and fall”</i>
(Luke <b>2</b>:34).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        More
broadly speaking, <i>CV</i> has re-sequenced entries to match English custom. The
New Testament tends to start with the main or most important and work down or
add in the lesser. A prime example is in the Parable of the Sower: <i>“hundred,
sixty, thirtyfold” </i>changes to <i>“thirty, sixty, a hundredfold”</i> (Matthew
<b>13</b>:23). English custom favors working up to the highest instead of
working down from it. Note also the following:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Matthew<b>
6</b>:29 (<i>toil</i> and <i>spin </i>reversed); <b>23</b>:27; Mark<b> 12</b>:8;
John<b> 20</b>:27; 1 Timothy <b>4</b>:11; Revelation <b>5</b>:6; <b>21</b>:5;<b>
22</b>:12.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Matthew
<b>7</b>:13: <i>“broad road and wide gate”</i> instead of <i>“wide gate and
broad road”</i>; travelers use the road before they enter a gate.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Matthew
<b>18</b>:17: <i>“dead men’s bones and filth” </i>becomes <i>“filth and dead
men’s bones”</i>; that sequence puts the bigger word group second.                        </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Mark
<b>6</b>:40: <i>“sit in hundreds and fifties”</i> changes to <i>“fifties and
hundreds.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Luke
<b>6</b>:1: <i>“</i></span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>his disciples were picking off heads,
rubbing them in their hands, and eating the seeds” </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>changed
from <i>“his disciples were picking off heads, eating the seeds, rubbing them
with their hands.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Acts
<b>10</b>:12 speaks of <i>“four-footed animals, reptiles, and birds.”</i> The
problem for English is that an adjective on the lead noun can feel like it
belongs to the next one(s) too. There cannot be any four-footed birds; so the
reversed sequence lists the <i>“four-footed animals” </i>last. </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Similarly,
in Hebrews <b>9</b>:19, choosing to say <i>“young bulls”</i> instead of <i>“calves”</i>
calls for reversing the order to read <i>“goats and young bulls.”</i> Luke <b>8</b>:2
says some women were “<i>healed from evil spirits and illnesses,”</i> which
becomes <i>“healed from illnesses and evil spirits.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        2
Corinthians <b>8</b>:10: <i>“A year ago you began to want to do and to do”</i>
changed from <i>“A year ago you began to do and want to do”</i>; wanting
precedes doing.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Revelation<b>
18</b>:8: <i>“death, mourning, and famine”</i> reversed;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Revelation<b>
21</b>:4: <i>“death, weeping, crying, or pain”</i> re-sequenced to <i>“pain,
weeping, crying, or death.” </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Greek
and English differ greatly in their possibilities for sentence structure. English
depends on placement in the sentence (distribution) to indicate subject, direct
object, indirect object, and modifiers. Greek uses case endings instead. That
leaves distribution for other uses like emphasis and easier connection with
preceding material. The most prominent places in sentences are the front and
end. Greek can play on that fact by putting, say, the direct object on the
front of a sentence or by placing the verb at the end. English is usually understandable
with a direct object ahead of the subject, but it is not customary to put it
there: <i>“My house I painted last summer.” </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        So,
one unavoidable loss in translation is nuance, as in emphasis. The loss does
not usually change the semantic meaning—the substance of expression, but it is
a small loss, nonetheless. That matter is prominent in conversational
re-expression. To compensate for the loss, a reader can consult the wooden or
classical translations available on this website.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Also
note #34 below.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            16.
<u>Noun-Pronoun Shifts</u>. Pronouns have replaced nouns and <i>vice versa</i>
for clarity, efficiency of expression, and eliminating repetition: Matthew <b>3</b>:16;
Mark <b>9</b>:50; Luke <b>23</b>:46; John <b>6</b>:24; <b>21</b>:13; Revelation
<b>14</b>:14-15.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            17.
<u>Preference for Positive Expression</u>. Written English prefers positive
expression: Luke <b>1</b>:37; <b>8</b>:17; <b>12</b>:2; <b>18</b>:29. <i>“Nothing
will be impossible for you”</i> changes to <i>“You can do anything.”</i> Note
also Mathew <b>12</b>:24. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Hebrew
idiom often states the positive plus the negative as perhaps a way to emphasize
or clarify. <i>CV</i> sometimes reduces double expressions to positive ones:
Ephesians <b>5</b>:15, <i>“Live like wise people”</i> rather than <i>“not live
like unwise but as wise.”</i> Cp. Matthew <b>13</b>:34; Mark <b>4</b>:18, <i>etc.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            18.
<u>Litotes Removed</u>. Most cases of <u>litotes</u> sound less natural to
conversational style, and so have fallen out: Matthew <b>15</b>:24; <b>17</b>:20;
Mark<b> 6</b>:4; Acts <b>21</b>:39 (<i>“No mean city” </i>becomes <i>“an
important city.”</i>); Titus<b> 3</b>:14.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5pt;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:0in;text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *19. <u>Removing
Body-Part Imagery</u>. English uses a certain amount of body-part terminology: <i>“how
did you get that in your head?” “give me a hand with this problem,” “keep your
nose out of my business.” CV</i> removes a high percentage of body-part
expression as scripture uses them. Though understandable, they are more foreign
to English idiom, but they are exceptionally frequent in the Bible. That Hebrew
manner of speaking <u>focuses action</u>: <i>“The feet of them that buried your
husband are at the door”</i> (Acts<b> 5</b>:9). Some examples are part of
pleonastic expression that <u>emphasizes action</u>: <i>“He opened his mouth
and said,”</i> instead of simply, <i>“He said.”</i> The Hebrew idiom can give <u>concrete
expression</u> to abstractions: Matthew <b>26</b>:50; John <b>16</b>:22; Acts <b>11</b>:21,
22, 30; <b>17</b>:26; Revelation <b>1</b>:4 (<i>“Everyone/<s>eye</s> will see
him.”</i>). Some body-part examples belong too much to the warp and woof of the
text to eliminate them: <i>“every knee . . . would bend, and every tongue would
agree”</i> (Philippians <b>2</b>:18). Note Matthew <b>13</b>:15 = Acts<b> 28</b>:27;
Romans <b>3</b>:13-15 (&lt; Psalm <b>5</b>:9; <b>140</b>:3; <b>10</b>:7); 1
Peter <b>3</b>:10-13 (&lt; Psalm <b>34</b>:12-16); Revelation <b>13</b>:5. A
few are too expressive to replace appropriately: <i>“Mary treasured these
things up <u>in her heart</u>”</i> (Luke <b>2</b>:19, 51; cp. <b>2</b>:35).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            20.
<u>Possessive Adjectives for the Greek Article</u>. It is not that the Greek
article <u>means</u> possessive adjective; Greek <u>custom of usage</u> is satisfied
to use the article when the possessor is otherwise clear. English wants to <u>say</u>
it regardless. Mark <b>4</b>:29 (<i>his</i> scythe);<b> 7</b>:3 (wash <i>their</i>
hands); 2 Peter<b> 3</b>:16. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            21.
<u>Dropping Dative of Possession</u>. <u>Have</u> replaces it: <i>“There is a
flock to the man”</i> <i>vs.</i> <i>“The man has a flock.”</i> Luke says of
Zachariah and Elizabeth, <i>“No child was to them”</i> (Luke <b>1</b>:7); that
can become <i>“they didn’t have any children.”</i> Cp. Luke <b>4</b>:7; John <b>1</b>:6.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Dative
of possession remains in texts like 1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:57: <i>“Thanks be
to God”</i>; <i>“Thanks to God.”</i> The <i>“have”</i> wording does not cover
the same idea in such a case.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            22.
<u>Avoiding Third Person Imperatives</u>. Greek third-person imperative forms have
no exact parallel in English. English imperative forms exist only in the second
person. <i>“<u>Let</u> him/her/it”</i> is the closest English construction; <i>“let”</i>
does not mean <i>“allow”</i> in that formula, so it can have an imperative value.
Today’s English does not readily use that idiom, so we have substituted <i>should</i>,
<i>ought</i>, or something similar. Though it is not the exact same meaning,
that is what we would say—a kind of moral “oughtness.” 1 Corinthians <b>3</b>:18,
21, <i>“Don’t anybody . . . ”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            23.
<u>Reduced Sentence Length</u>. Considerations noted above include reducing
sentence length. Several devices help achieve that end. To an English reader,
the classical biblical translations feel burdened down with
wordiness—tautology, redundancy, circumlocution, verbosity, pleonastic
expression. To English speakers, that factor reduces interest and clarity. Examples
of wordiness: John <b>5</b>:25; 1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:8-9; 2 Corinthians <b>3</b>:14-15;
<b>11</b>:21; <b>12</b>:3. Reduced sentence length comes from using several of
the mechanisms noted above: </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        making
two sentences by omitting coordinate—and sometimes subordinate—conjunctions
between them: Luke <b>1</b>:1-4; Ephesians <b>1</b>:3-14; <b>2</b>:8; <b>5</b>:21;
Hebrews <b>1</b>:1-4; James <b>1</b>:5-8; 2 Peter <b>2</b>:4-9; Jude 12-15;
Revelation <b>14</b>:5 (at the end);   </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        replacing
participial phrases with full verbs to create clauses; </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        omitting
some whole verses to keep the thought moving more quickly: Matthew <b>24</b>:25;
John <b>9</b>:23; 2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:3; <b>12</b>:3; Ephesians <b>4</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        dropping
<i>for</i> and <i>because</i> in several instances. <i>For</i> (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>γάρ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
is often illative anyway; that is, it makes a connection with the preceding
comment, but it is not a logical connection or causal sequence. So to speak, <i>CV</i>
has weakened significantly the dictum, <i>“When you see a ‘therefore,’ see what
it is there for”</i>; the saying does not necessarily hold true. See 2 Timothy <b>2</b>:7:</span><b><sup><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span></sup></b><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Think
about what I’m saying; <s>for</s> the Lord will help you understand.” </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Cp.
John <b>7</b>:29.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        eliminating
nuancing particles that Greek sprinkles into sentences quite understandable
without them: </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄρα</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>, </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>γὲ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>μὲν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
. . . </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>δὲ</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>, </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>μὴν</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>often,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πὲρ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>νή</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.
Compounds with </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τοὶ</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>, </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πέρ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κέν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
are treated as if they were not affixed to the word;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        replacing
<i>and</i>’s with commas between list items; replacing <i>and’s</i> with
periods—or perhaps semi-colons—between what can become independent clauses.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        getting
rid of “genitive strings” </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        dropping
redundancies caused by extra words like these in Revelation: <i>“held palm
branches <s>in their hands”</s></i> (<b>7</b>:9, <i>“held”</i> covers the
prepositional phrase as well); <i>“dressed in white <s>clothes</s>”</i> (<b>4</b>:4);
<i>“the first looked like a lion, the second <s>looked </s>like a calf” </i>(<b>4</b>:7);
“<i>He gave each one <s>of them</s> a white robe”</i> (<b>6</b>:11); <i>“till
their fellow <s>servants that were going to be martyred/</s> martyrs would
fulfill their course”</i> (<b>6</b>:11); <i>“coming on the clouds <s>of the sky</s>”</i>
(Matthew <b>26</b>:64; dropping the prepositional phrase does interfere with
the parallelism <i>“sitting on the right hand <u>of Power</u>”</i>)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        dividing
long sentences into sets of shorter ones. Long sentences show up notably in the
longer prison letters, as an English reader can see by looking at the <i>American
Standard Version</i> (1901): Ephesians <b>1</b>:3-14, 15-23; <b>2</b>:1-10,
11-18, 20-22; <b>3</b>:1-12, 14-19; <b>4</b>:1-3, 4-6, 11-16, 17-24; <b>5</b>:7-12,
18-21, 25-27; <b>6</b>:5-8, 14-16, 17-20; Philippians <b>1</b>:3-7, 9-11,
12-14, 15-17, 27-30; <b>2</b>:1-4, 5-8, 9-11, 12-13, 14-16, 25-27; <b>3</b>:2-6;
Colossians<b> 1</b>:3-8, 9-17, 18-20, 21-23, 24-29;<b> 2</b>:8-12, 13-15. See
also 2 Peter 2:4-10a.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            24.
<u>Asyndetic Construction</u>. English tends toward sentences not connected by
conjunctions; Greek and Hebrew have much the opposite bent. <i>CV</i> has omitted
as many coordinating conjunctions as possible, particularly conjunctive ones (<i>and</i>).
As noted earlier, it even omits <u>because</u> in a good many places; English expects
the hearer to perceive that the next sentence expresses the reason for the
preceding one when it has that possibility. (These last two sentences illustrate
the phenomenon.) Asyndetic construction also shortens sentences a little.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            25.
<u>Purpose Clauses</u>. Purpose clauses shift into infinitive phrases<i>: “in
order that he might ___”</i> shortens to <i>“to ___.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            26.
<u>English Present Perfect for Greek Aorist</u>. Both languages have both
verbal aspects: action in the past connected with present time (perfect; <i>“has
gone”</i>; linear) and simple reference to the fact of past action (aorist; <i>“went”</i>).
(Also, the Greek imperfect views past action as in progress: <i>“was going”</i>;
linear). The real action itself can be the same in all these cases, of course,
but it is viewed differently by the writer for communication purposes—emphasis,
vividness, relationship to other actions, what the author wants to draw
attention to, habit of expression, <i>etc.</i> So, shifting from one to the
other does not alter the fact, but it does change the way the writer talks about
it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Although
both English and Greek have <u>perfect and aoristic aspects in their verbs</u>,
they do not have the same <u>custom of usage</u> with them. English more often
wants to connect the past act to the present time, that is, it wants to use the
present perfect rather than the simple past (aorist); Greek tends the other way.
The difference shows up famously in a couple texts that are part of theological
discussion: Romans <b>3</b>:23;<b> 5</b>:12. <i>CV</i> translates both aorists
as present perfects to match the preferred understanding of Paul’s doctrinal
intent: <i>“Everybody <u>has sinned</u> and falls short of God’s glory”</i>; <i>“death
passed on to everybody because everybody <u>has sinned</u>.”</i> The punctiliar
wording <i>“all sinned”</i> is easier to mistake into meaning all sinned in
Adam’s act of sinning because he was all the race—Augustine’s notion of federal
guilt in federal man—whereas guilt is, by nature and revelation, personal and
limited to the actor. (Now of course, one person can be thought of as, so to
speak, guilty of another person’s action by sympathizing with it, an idea
included in the Christ-Adam parallels Paul talks about: Romans <b>5</b>:14; 1
Corinthians <b>15</b>:22, 43.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            27.
<u>Preference for Action Verbs</u>. Action verbs take precedence over nouns,
adjectives, and <i>“is”</i> expressions. <i>“After his suffering”</i> can
become <i>“after he suffered</i> (Acts <b>1</b>:16).<i>”</i> Cp. 1 Corinthians <b>5</b>:5;
<b>8</b>:7; <b>14</b>:21 (<i>“I have no <u>need</u> of you” </i>becomes <i>“I
don’t <u>need</u> you”</i>); Hebrews <b>10</b>:26, 35.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            28.
<u>Continuity of Person</u>, To keep the same grammatical person as subject throughout
a segment, <i>CV</i> changes the original person when the shift does not alter
the point: Matthew <b>6</b>:24; <b>13</b>:14; Mark <b>7</b>:18-23; Luke <b>1</b>:44-45;
John <b>3</b>:36; <b>5</b>:24; <b>12</b>:40 &lt; Isaiah <b>6</b>:10; John <b>15</b>:5-6;
Acts <b>17</b>:15; <b>28</b>:27-28; Romans <b>13</b>:11; 1 Corinthians <b>3</b>:19;
1<b>4</b>:15-17, 23-24, 27-28; 2 Corinthians <b>4</b>:16-<b>5</b>:6; Hebrews <b>4</b>:1;
James <b>1</b>:22-27; Revelation <b>18</b>:19-20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            29.
<u>Continuity of Figure</u>. <i>CV</i> goes as far as “unmixing” a few <u>mixed
metaphors</u>. They seem unnecessarily distracting. 2 Corinthians<b> 5</b>:2 changes
from <i>“undressed”</i> to <i>“unhoused.”</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            30.
<u>Continuity of Tense</u>. Time viewpoint presents another translation issue. Mark
<b>8</b>:6 illustrates a tense shift that <i>CV </i>covers up: <i>“And he </i>instructs<i>
the crowd to sit down on the ground; and having taken the seven loaves and
having blessed them, he <u>gave</u> . . . .” </i>The new wording becomes, <i>“He
<u>told</u> the crowd to sit down. He took the seven loaves, blessed them, and <u>gave</u>
. . . .”</i> Mark’s gospel lends itself to many such shifts because Mark so
frequently uses the “historic present,” a practice called “vivid narration.” The
device uses a present-tense verb to put the listener inside the past-time
event. English speakers talk the same way, but it has a “colloquial” feel more
common in oral exchange. Cp. Matthew <b>2</b>:13.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            31.
<u>Close Subject-Verb Connection</u>. <i>CV</i> endeavors to limit the number
of words between subject and verb (and verb plus direct objects). Spoken English
can tolerate more words there than written speech can, because pitch pattern
and phrasing can help establish the connections. One blight in newspaper and
magazine writing of late is attempting to say too many things about the subject
before introducing its verb. Holding all that information in mental abeyance
while anticipating what the verb will be when it comes into the picture is
maddening! (See!) 2 Peter <b>2</b>:4-8 finally gets its verb in <b>2</b>:9! In
English, rearrangement and breakdown into smaller units seem to be in order.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            32.
<u>Double Entries</u>. New Testament writers show the Hebrew penchant for
double entry. In some cases, <i>CV</i> cuts such entries in half, especially if
the double comes from the following:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (a)
<u>affirming a fact plus denying its opposite</u>. Proverbs<b> 31</b>:12
illustrates the idea in its familiar description of an ideal wife: <i>“She will
do him good <u>and not evil</u> all her life.”</i> 2 Timothy <b>4</b>:16 says, <i>“Nobody
stood by me . . . . everybody deserted me.” “Is it lawful to pay tribute to
Caesar <s>or not</s>?”</i> (Matthew <b>22</b>:17 = Mark <b>12</b>:14 = Luke <b>20</b>:22).
Cp. Luke <b>18</b>:16 (<i>“Let them come over to me. . . </i><s>and don’t get
in their way</s>”)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    Cp.
Matthew <b>6</b>:13; <b>21</b>:21; Mark<b> 4</b>:33-34; Luke <b>14</b>:3; <b>16</b>:13;<b>
18</b>:15; John <b>1</b>:3;<b> 3</b>:20; <b>20</b>:27; Acts <b>1</b>:4; 2
Corinthians <b>13</b>:8; Ephesians <b>5</b>:15; 1 Timothy <b>2</b>:9; Hebrews<b>
1</b>:9; <b>13</b>:5.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (b)
Sometimes the double entry simply <u>emphasizes with synonyms</u> or
restatement: <i>“grief and constant sorrow”</i> (Romans <b>9</b>:2<i>; “trespasses
and sins”</i> (Ephesians<b> 2</b>:1). <i>“Where will the ungodly and sinner
appear?”</i> becomes <i>“where will godless people come in”</i> (1 Peter <b>4</b>:18)?
Luke <b>8</b>:1 has <i>“proclaiming and preaching”</i> (</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>κηρύσσω</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>εὐαγγελίζομαι</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>).
Matthew’s account of Jesus in Gethsemane has him beginning to <i>“feel stressed
and be in distress” </i>(</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>λυπεῖσθαι</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>καὶ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἀδημονεῖν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,</span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 26</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:37).
The close meanings provide different word pictures that deepen intensity; the <i>CV
wording</i> is <i>“</i></span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>became overwhelmed with dread.”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
himself expresses the experience as <i>“distressed to death”</i> (Matthew <b>26</b>:38).
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
Hebrew writer describes the occasion as <i>“loud</i> [audible?] <i>crying and
tears”</i> (Hebrews<b> 5</b>:7 <i>NASB</i>). Luke <b>8</b>:17 says, <i>“secret
that will not be known and come to light,”</i> which, reordered to natural
sequence, becomes <i>“secret that will not come to light and be known,”</i>
which, with double entry removed, becomes <i>“everything secret will come to
light <s>and be known</s>.”</i> </span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“Everything that’s
possible is not necessarily beneficial <s>or helpful</s>”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(1 Corinthians <b>10</b>:23).</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> One notable verse has a <u>triple</u>
entry: <i>“The</i> disciples<i> did not understand any of that, and the saying
was hidden from them. They did not comprehend what was said”</i> (Luke <b>18</b>:34).
The disciples really did not get it! <i>CV </i>reduces their response to <i>“None
of that made any sense to them whatever.” </i>Cp. John <b>11</b>:33; <b>15</b>:5.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (c)
A third kind of double entry is <u>hendiadys</u>, where two grammatically
parallel nouns, verbs, or adjectives combine for one composite idea: <i>“authoritative
rule/ruling authority”</i> for <i>“rule and authority”</i> (Luke<b> 20</b>:20).
Paul <i>“was still breathing out threats and murder” </i>becomes <i>“was still making
murderous threats” </i>(Acts<b> 9</b>:1). <i>“Spoke in languages and
prophesied”</i> likely means <i>“they prophesied in languages”</i> (Acts <b>19</b>:6).
<i>“A wide and effective door has opened up for me”</i> (1 Corinthians <b>16</b>:9).
<i>“We have boldness and access”</i> probably equals “<i>we have bold access” </i>(Ephesians
<b>3</b>:12). <i>“With the command and shout of the archangel”</i> becomes <i>“with
the shout of the archangel”</i> (1 Thessalonians <b>4</b>:16). Simon in Samaria
was impressed with Philip’s <i>“outstanding signs and miracles”</i>; <i>CV</i>
combines the two into “miraculous signs,” meaning the <u>supernatural acts</u>
that <u>signified</u> something; they were not sorcery like Simon the
sorcerer’s demonstrations, sheer display with nothing more than entertainment
value like magicians and illusionists do.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
