style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (d)
A fourth kind of double entry seems to be a <u>rhetorical device</u> for
balance or emphasis: <i>“a merciful and faithful high priest”</i> (Hebrews <b>2</b>:17),
<i>“the Apostle and High Priest of our confession”</i> (Hebrews <b>3</b>:1), <i>“hold
fast our boldness and glorying firm to the end” </i>(Hebrews <b>3</b>:6; both
are true, and they are not the same idea, but why say them both here except for
rhetorical balance?). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (e)
In a few examples, the double seems to be <u>customary expression</u>, as in
“eat and drink,” which <i>CV</i> sometimes reduces to <i>“eat” </i>(Matthew <b>24</b>:38;
Luke <b>5</b>:30, 33; <b>10</b>:7; <b>13</b>:26; <b>17</b>:8<sup>2</sup>; <b>17</b>:27,
28; <b>22</b>:30; 1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:4; <b>11</b>:22.). That has not
happened in Matthew <b>6</b>:25, 31; <b>11</b>:18, 19; <b>24</b>:49; Luke <b>7</b>:33,
34; <b>12</b>:19, 29, 45; John <b>6</b>:53-54, 56; Acts <b>9</b>:9; <b>23</b>:12,
21; Romans <b>14</b>:21; 1 Corinthians <b>10</b>:7, 31; <b>15</b>:32, because
both eating and drinking are integral to the writer’s point. Note also Matthew <b>5</b>:6,
<i>“Hungry and thirsty for righteousness.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <i>“Marry
and given in marriage”</i> can reduce to <i>“get married”</i>: Matthew <b>22</b>:30;
<b>24</b>:38; Mark <b>12</b>:25; Luke <b>20</b>:34, 35. The shift fits better
the modern Western marriage custom where there is one action: the couple’s
decision to marry, instead of one set of parents searching for a suitable
in-law and the other parents agreeing to give a daughter in marriage.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <i>“See
and hear”</i> becomes <i>“see”</i> in some cases: Luke<b> 2</b>:20; <b>7</b>:22;
2 Corinthians </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    <b>2</b>:6.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <i>“Flesh
and blood”</i> means <i>“people </i>(in this world)<i>”</i> in contrast to God
as spirit: Matthew <b>16</b>:17 (<i>“Flesh and blood did not reveal that to
you”</i>); 1 Corinthians<b> 15</b>:50, <i>etc.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <i>“Fear
and trembling”</i> (1 Corinthians <b>2</b>:3; 2 Corinthians <b>7</b>:15;
Ephesians <b>6</b>:5; Philippians <b>2</b>:12; cp. Psalm <b>55</b>:5) seems too
strong for some circumstances where it appears. Perhaps the set expression had
become so familiar that it lost some of its intensity.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <i>“Day
and night”</i> for <i>“all the time,”</i> <i>“every day,”</i> continually,
characteristically: </span><sup><span style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>+</span></sup><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 4</b>:2 (Jesus
fasted forty days <s>and nights</s>); <b>12</b>:40 (Jonah in the stomach of the
big fish three days and nights); Mark <b>4</b>:27 (<i>“day after day”</i>); <b>5</b>:5;
Luke <b>2</b>:37; <b>18</b>:7; Acts <b>9</b>:24; <b>20</b>:31; <b>26</b>:7 (<i>“every
day</i>”); 1 Thessalonians<b> 2</b>:9; <b>3</b>:10; 2 Thessalonians <b>3</b>:8;
1 Timothy<b> 5</b>:5; 2 Timothy <b>1</b>:3; Revelation <b>4</b>:8; <b>7</b>:15;
<b>12</b>:10; <b>14</b>:11; <b>20</b>:10). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    In
the case of Jesus’ being in the ground three days and nights like Jonah, it is
not possible for that to fit literally with <i>“resurrect the third day”</i> (Matthew
<b>16</b>:21; <b>17</b>:23; <b>27</b>:64; Mark <b>9</b>:31; <b>10</b>:34; Luke <b>9</b>:22;
<b>13</b>:22; <b>20</b>:12; <b>24</b>:7). He was crucified in the daytime and
was to arise the third day. No combination of <i>day-night </i>ending in <i>day</i>
will get the same number of nights as days—three and three. The statement would
have to change the meaning of <i>day</i> to <i>twenty-four-hour day</i> for the
last “day” to make the statement work at all, but the prophecy in Matthew <b>12</b>:40
says <i>“three days and three nights.” </i>The Jonah-Jesus comparison could
work if (in the Jonah <u>statement</u> of the comparison) the <u>accounts</u>
mean to say Jesus resurrected while it was still dark (sometime before the
fourth day). But understood that way, the accounts themselves do not fit the
other <u>wording</u> about his resurrecting the third day. Besides, Matthew <b>28</b>:1;
Mark <b>16</b>:2; Luke <b>24</b>:1 say early dawn while John<b> 20</b>:1 says <i>‘it
was dark”</i> when the women came.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (f)
<i>“word and deed”</i>: Acts <b>7</b>:22; Romans <b>15</b>:18; Colossians <b>3</b>:17;
(1 John<b> 3</b>:18)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (g)
<i>“miracles and wonders and signs”</i>: Matthew <b>24</b>:24; Mark <b>13</b>:22;
Acts <b>2</b>:19, 22, 43; <b>4</b>:30; <b>5</b>:12; <b>6</b>:8; <b>7</b>:36; <b>14</b>:3;
<b>15</b>:12; Romans <b>15:</b>19; 2 Corinthians<b> 12</b>:12; 2 Thessalonians<b>
2</b>:9; Hebrews <b>2</b>:4.  <i>Miracles</i> could relate to being above
natural causation; <i>wonders</i> could speak to the impact on observers; <i>signs</i>
could reference the meaningfulness of the event; on the other hand, the triple
could simply be a strong emphatic.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (h)
Other kinds of double entries show up in 1 Corinthians <b>5</b>:8 (malice and
wickedness, sincerity and truth); Ephesians <b>3</b>:20 (ask or think); James <b>1</b>:17
(<i>every good and perfect gift</i>); 1 Timothy <b>6</b>:9 (<i>plunges them
into ruin <s>and destruction</s></i>). Especially with noun synonyms based on
different word pictures, double entries are a way to create degrees with nouns
comparable to comparative or superlative degrees on adjectives. Sometimes the
doubles are longer segments laid side by side, not standard expressions. They
represent rhetorical custom of expression—like some speakers’ habit of speaking
in threes. Reading Romans, <i>etc.</i>, with an eye for doublets shows how
prevalent that habit of expression was.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                     </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            33.
<u>Avoiding Cognate Accusatives</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        New
Testament authors may use a verb plus a direct object from the same root (or
equivalent expression), as in <i>“I’ve fought a good fight”</i> (2 Timothy <b>4</b>:7).
Timothy <i>“is working the work of the Lord”</i> (1 Corinthians <b>16</b>:10),
reworded, becomes, <i>“He’s doing the Lord’s work.”</i> Matthew<b> 2</b>:10
says <i>“they rejoiced an exceedingly great joy”</i> becomes <i>“they were
overjoyed.”</i> Cp. Luke <b>1</b>:73;<b> 2</b>:8, 9; Acts <b>19</b>:4; 1 Peter <b>5</b>:2.
The cognate accusative is often a Hebraism for intensity: Revelation <b>18</b>:6
(</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>διπλώσατε</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τὰ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>διπλᾶ</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
pay her back double). Some, of course, <i>CV </i>lets stand as the classic
double cognate in Ephesians <b>4</b>:8 &lt; Psalm <b>68</b>:18, <i>“captured
captivity and gave gifts to people.”</i> There are a few <i>“near cognate
accusatives”</i> like Ephesians <b>4</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            34.
<u>Keeping All Parts of Indirect Discourse Together</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        New
Testament writers sometimes state part of the indirect discourse portion ahead
of the conjunction that leads into the noun clause. That part comes up again in
the indirect discourse portion. 1 Corinthians <b>16</b>:15 says, for example, <i>“You
know the household of Stephanas—that it is the first fruits of Achaia”</i> (comparable
to double subject). Adjusted, the sentence becomes, <i>“You know that the
household of Stephanas was the first fruits of Achaia.”</i> See also Matthew <b>26</b>:10,
24; Mark <b>14</b>:6; John <b>6</b>:28; <b>9</b>:4; Acts <b>13</b>:41.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
1.0in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>A
similar repetition ahead of the main construction happens with relative
clauses: “</span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>I’ve seen your salvation, which you prepared in front
of all nations” </span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(Luke <b>2</b>:30). <i>CV</i> words it, </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“</span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>I’ve
seen the salvation you’ve prepared for all nations.”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
See this recurring pleonastic feature with pronouns in Revelation <b>2</b>:17;<b>
3</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
1.0in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>A
similar double possessive happens in the phrase “<u>her</u> passion of her
immorality,” which gets changed to “her immoral passion” (Revelation <b>14</b>:8).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            35.
<u>Avoiding Absolute Constructions</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        New
Testament Greek uses lots of absolute constructions, that is, constructions
that are not by grammar tied to elements in the main clause. It occurs quite
often as illustrated by the cases just in Matthew: <b>1</b>:18, 20; <b>2</b>:1,
13, 19; <b>6</b>:3; <b>8</b>:16, 28;<b> 9</b>:32, 33; <b>11</b>:7; <b>12</b>:46;
<b>13</b>:6, 19; <b>14</b>:15, 23, 32; <b>16</b>:2; <b>17</b>:5, 9, 14, 22, 24,
26; <b>18</b>:24, 25; <b>20</b>:8, 29; <b>21</b>:10, 23; <b>22</b>:41; <b>24</b>:3;
<b>25</b>:5, 10, 60; <b>26</b>:6, 7, 20, 21, 47; <b>27</b>:1, 17, 19, 57; <b>28</b>:11,
13. There are a few accusative absolutes: Matthew <b>17</b>:25?; Acts <b>25</b>:27;
<b>26</b>:3; Ephesians <b>1</b>:18. Some dative absolutes show up: Matthew<b> 9</b>:27,
28?; <b>14</b>:6; Luke <b>8</b>:27; Acts <b>22</b>:6, 17; <b>28</b>:10?. There are
nominative absolutes in Romans <b>12</b>:9, 16; Revelation <b>2</b>:26. (Latin has
ablative absolutes). The construction consists of a genitive/dative/accusative/nominative
noun plus the same number-and-gender participle without modifying any
substantive in the main clause. Mark <b>1</b>:32, for example, says, <i>“And evening
having come, . . . they were bringing him . . .</i> (</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ὀψίας</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>δὲ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>γενομένης</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
. . . </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>ἔφερον</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πρὸς</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>αὐτὸν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
. . . ).” Using the English nominative absolute, as we have done in this
example, offers a comparable wording still encountered in English in short, set
expressions like <i>“That said, . . . .”</i>; <i>“weather permitting, . . .”</i>
The Mark<b> 1</b>:32 example we simplified to <i>“That evening.”</i> At other
times a full adverbial clause works, <i>“when evening came.”</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            36.
<u>Deliberative Questions</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <u>Deliberative
questions</u> imply uncertainty about what to do together perhaps with an
element of intensity or urgency. The nuance is not easy to indicate noticeably in
the English wording; so we have resorted to </span><sup><span style='font-size:
14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>+</span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>special
punctuation in a few cases (which replaces voice modulation in oral
presentation): <i>“What are we going to eat!?”</i> (Matthew<b> 6</b>:31); <i>“What
should I ask for!?”</i> (Mark <b>6</b>:24; <i>CV </i>hides the deliberation in
indirect question); <i>“Should we go spend two hundred denarii on bread to feed
them!?”</i> (Mark <b>6</b>:37); <i>“Should we strike with a sword!?”</i> (Luke <b>22</b>:49);
<i>“What are we going to do!?” </i>(Acts <b>2</b>:37); </span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“You
want me to crucify your king!?” </span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(John<b> 19</b>:15);<i> </i></span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“What
are we going to do with these men!?”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> (Acts <b>4</b>:15); <i>“What
should I do, Lord!?”</i> (Acts <b>22</b>:10). Some questions with subjunctive seem
to have weaker uncertainty, so special punctuation does not accompany them. Examples
include Matthew <b>3</b>:14?; <b>6</b>:25, 37; <b>13</b>:28; <b>18</b>:21; <b>23</b>:33;
<b>26</b>:14, 17, 54; Mark <b>12</b>:14; Luke <b>3</b>:10;<b> 9</b>:54; <b>11</b>:5;
<b>12</b>:5; <b>14</b>:34; <b>16</b>:3-4;<b> 18</b>:7, 41; <b>22</b>:9; <b>23</b>:31;
John<b> 6</b>:28, 38, 68;<b> 12</b>:27; <b>18</b>:11, 39; Romans <b>10</b>:14; <b>15</b>:4;
1 Corinthians <b>4</b>:21; <b>11</b>:22; Hebrews <b>11</b>:32; Revelation <b>15</b>:4.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            37.
<u>Actual <i>vs.</i> Tendential Result</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        The
classical Greek distinction between actual result (<i>so that</i>) and
tendential result (<i>so as to</i>) is not sharply maintained in the New
Testament (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὥστε</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>+
indicative <i>vs.</i> </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὥστε</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>+
infinitive). We have not always worded the texts with a clear preference for
either. See comments below under <i>so</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Our
comments on the “wordiness” of biblical expression observes <u>custom of usage</u>,
not literary competence. Some of these usages amount to mechanisms for emphasis,
which vary across languages. In written material especially, English likes to emphasize
with short, quick, pregnant expression; New Testament authors seem to emphasize
by saying lots of words (cp. Matthew <b>6</b>:7!). 1 Corinthians <b>4</b>:16 illustrates<i>:
“I urge you to be imitators of me.”</i> It sounds more natural to say, <i>“Imitate
me.”</i> The urging part comes from using the imperative mood; <i>“imitate”</i>
is an action verb with greater punch than <i>“to be”</i> plus a noun <i>“imitator.”</i>
Redundancy and restatement disappear by simple omission: Luke <b>11</b>:36
(dropping <i>“not having any part dark”</i>). Note Philippians <b>2</b>:21. Note
also the [<sup>x</sup>] and [<sup>x</sup>]<sup>repeat</sup> sigla at the
beginning of these notes. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            38.
<u>Third Person for First-Person Reference</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Using
third-person for first-person reference grates on English expectancies, but <i>CV</i>
has retained it in a majority of Jesus’ <i>“Son of Man”</i> usages instead of
changing it to <i>I </i>or <i>me. </i>We have supposed he did it deliberately to
identify himself with the <i>“Son of Man”</i> expression in Daniel <b>7</b>:13
as an oblique claim to be the Messiah<i>: “One like a son of man came up to the
Ancient One”</i> (Matthew <b>8</b>:20; <b>9</b>:6, 23; <b>11</b>:19; <b>12</b>:40;
<b>13</b>:37, 42; <b>16</b>:13, 27<sup>2</sup>; <b><s>17</s></b><s>:12;</s> 1<b>8</b>:28;
<b>24</b>:27, 30, 37, 39,44; <b>25</b>:31; <b>26</b>:24; Mark<b> 2</b>:10;<b> 8</b>:31;
<b>9</b>:9, 12, 30; <b>10</b>:33, 45; <b>13</b>:<u>26</u>, 32; <b>14</b>:20,
41, <u>62</u>; Luke <b>5</b>:24; <b>6</b>:22; <b>7</b>:34; <b>9</b>:22, 26, 44,
58; <b>11</b>:30; <b>12</b>:40; <b>17</b>:22, 24, 26, 30; <b>18</b>:8, 31; <b>19</b>:10;
<b>21</b>:<u>27</u>, 36; <b>22</b>:70; John <b>1</b>:51;<b> 3</b>:13, 14;<b> 5</b>:19;<b>
6</b>:27, 53, 62; <b>8</b>:28; <b>9</b>:35; <b>12</b>:23, 34, 35; <b>13</b>:31<sup>2</sup>;
Acts <b>7</b>:56; <b>8</b>:20. All but two appear in the gospels. <i>CV</i> makes
the preferred reading in Mark <b>2</b>:28 a general reference to people instead
of a specific one about Jesus.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><sup><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    </span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Farther
along in Jesus’ ministry especially, <i>CV</i> has replaced several
third-person <i>“Son of Man”</i> cases with first-person pronouns:  Matthew <b>12</b>:32;
<b>17</b>:9, 12, 22; <b>20</b>:18-19, 28; <b>26</b>:2, 45; Mark <b>8</b>:38; <b>10</b>:33,
45; Luke <b>12</b>:8, 10; <b>22</b>:22, 48; <b>24</b>:7</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><sup><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  </span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>39.
<u>Pronoun Replacement for Anaphoric Nouns</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        Under
the influence of Hebrew grandiloquence, New Testament writers sometimes restate
a noun (or clause) that English replaces with a pronoun.  Revelation <b>17</b>:8,
<i>The beast was, is not, and is to come up from the abyss to </i>its <i>eternal
destruction. People on earth will marvel when they see <u>it</u>. It</i> in <i>CV
</i>replaces a restatement of <i>“He was, is not, and will come” </i>from
earlier. Cp. John <b>2</b>:24; Colossians <b>1</b>:18, 20, 27.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            40.
In some cases, natural sequence has replaced the sequence of actions in the
texts: Acts <b>23</b>:32; Jude 1(?).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            41.
Comments above note Greek’s tendency to proliferate participial constructions,
which produce hypotactic arrangements. Hebrew idiom shows through in some
constructions joined by <i>and</i> where English prefers hypotactic
arrangement, particularly when the second verb fulfills the purpose of the
first. For example, Matthew <b>24</b>:31 says,</span><b><sup><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span></sup></b><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>“He’ll
send out his angels with a loud trumpet blast <u>and</u> gather his chosen ones
from every direction.” CV</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> reads, <i>“He’ll send out his angels
with a loud trumpet blast <u>to</u> gather his chosen ones.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;
display:none'> Hebrew grandiloqu3ence,gr</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Current
Shifts</span></b><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'> in</span></b><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> English Usage </span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            As
with Bible translations prepared since the 1940’s, the archaic pronouns (<i>thou/thee/thy,
ye, mine </i>in attributive position—<i>mine eyes</i>) and old verb endings (-<i>est,
-eth</i>) have dropped out, along with a host of words and phrases no longer in
use, though probably still understandable. Other words have shifted their
meaning and need to be replaced. (<i>Let, to hinder, </i>moved to its opposite!
KJV used to say in Romans <b>1</b>:13, <i>“I was let hitherto”</i> for <i>“I
was prevented up to now.”</i>) Linguistic drift happens in every language, so
new versions come out to accommodate the changes. Besides that, the impetus for
this translation comes from wanting to provide scripture in one kind of current
English. Its features appear above under “III. Elements of Conversational
Style.” It attempts to bring scripture closer to this generation’s oral style.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            1.
The <u>may/can distinction</u> falls aside—at least where a reader can easily
get the correct import (Revelation <b>4</b>:13). Today, <i>may</i> indicates
contingent action only; <i>can</i> has taken over its duties for expressing
permission. <i>“You may use the car”</i> sounds stilted, and so changes to, <i>“You
can use it.” </i>Note John <b>9</b>:39.                      </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            2.
Not surprisingly, <i>CV</i> also drops the <u>will/shall distinction</u>. For
the last couple generations English speakers have not used <i>shall</i> for
first person and <i>will</i> for second and third person—with reversal for
emphasis. Now, <i>will</i> covers all three persons and there is no emphasis by
reversal; <i>shall</i> falls out altogether. Calling attention to that obvious
shift would not be necessary except for familiar biblical texts like the Ten Commandments—<i>“Thou
shalt not steal.” “Do not steal”</i> gets rid of the archaic second person
singular pronoun <i>thou</i>, replaces imperatival future with plain
imperative, changes the obsolete form of second-person <i>shall</i> (<i>shalt</i>),
and eliminates the rare use of a pronoun with an imperative—except occasionally
for strong demand. <i>CV</i> uses contractions almost everywhere it can, but in
the Ten Commandments it leaves the words uncontracted, as in this example.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            
3. In general, we have deferred to “<u>inclusive language</u>.” Except in third
singular, English has common gender in all its <u>pronoun</u> types and their inflexions:
personal (<i>I/we/you/he/ she/they</i>), reflexive (-<i>self</i>), intensive (-<i>self</i>),
reciprocal (<i>one another</i>), interrogative (<i>who?/which?</i>), indefinite
(<i>some/any/every/body</i>), and relative (<i>who/which/that</i>). Classical
languages (Greek and Latin), Hebrew/Aramaic, and English speakers
(traditionally) have used <i>he</i>/<i>him</i>/<i>his</i> (and their corresponding
terms) where sex reference is mixed, unknown, or irrelevant. The modern concern
for egalitarianism has pressed for a shift away from masculine pronouns in those
situations. Written and oral custom has begun to foster using “<i>he or she</i>,”
mixing the two pronouns in a speech segment, or using <i>they/their</i> as a
replacement for singular third-person reference by resumptive pronouns (<i>“as
anyone who does it for their job can tell you”</i>). We have also not revived
the free-standing <i>one</i> to cover generic reference: <i>“One should not . .
. .”</i> The word has picked up a pedantic feel due to its infrequent usage in
that way.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            The
replacement options seem cumbersome, distracting, and ambiguous. Indefinite
pronouns and their equivalents are more natural: <i>some/body/any/body/everybody-one</i>,
<i>a person</i>, or <i>people</i>. It is possible as well to use <i>you</i> or <i>they</i>
“advisedly,” that is, as indefinite pronouns—as in the expression <i>“they say.”</i>
There is also editorial <i>we</i> and the use of plurals in place of singulars.
Passive voice can replace literal construction, but <i>CV</i> has tried to
avoid passive constructions. Where such indefinite reference seems forced, we
have reverted to traditional usage, as explained below. In Revelation <b>13</b>:16
we resort to <i>their</i> as a plural cover-up for <i>his</i> because of its
distributive sense: <i>“It made everybody<sup>sg</sup> . . . receive a stamp on
their<sup>pl</sup> forehead or right hand”</i>; note also Matthew <b>5</b>:39,
40, 41;<b> 7</b>:5;<b> 18</b>:6; Acts <b>2</b>:6; Romans <b>2</b>:6; 1
Corinthians <b>8</b>:10; 2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:16, 17; Colossians <b>2</b>:18,
2 Thessalonians <b>3</b>:10; 2 Timothy <b>2</b>:18; Revelation<b> 22</b>:12. Cp.
Mark <b>3</b>:29.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Using
indefinite pronouns, <i>etc</i>., to replace <i>man</i> does not solve the inclusive
language problem where resumptive pronouns come in; hence, the use of plurals
in the resumptive positions is becoming English idiom whether the leading
reference is singular or plural. The exception is that originating references
intended to specify one gender can carry the same gender resumptive pronoun. <i>CV
</i>implements the new idiom in cases like the following: <i>“Each one of us
has to carry our own load”</i> (Galatians <b>6</b>:5); <i>“whoever tries to
preserve their life will lose it”</i> (Luke <b>17</b>:33).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Inclusive
language also comes into play when the original text uses the <u>nouns</u> <i>son,
brother, father, man, and their plurals </i>for mixed referents<i>. </i>In <i>CV
</i>the word <i>man</i> appears where it contrasts with woman; but when it
contrasts with God, animal, idea, or inanimate thing we have sought some
alternative replacement. Where <i>man</i> does not contrast in those ways, </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄνθρωπος</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(<i>man</i>)—and
to a lesser extent </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὰνήρ</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> (<i>man/husband</i>)—we
have endeavored to replace with <i>a person</i>, <i>people</i>, plurals, first
or second person (Romans <b>10</b>:9-10), or some other inclusive term. In such
cases, <i>man</i> in the original text does not mean <i>“man as in contrast to
woman”</i> anyway; it means <i>humankind</i>, but <i>humankind</i> is a bit
cumbersome. <i>CV </i>has replaced <i>brothers </i>with <i>siblings </i>in<i> </i>Romans
<b>15</b>:14;<i> </i>Hebrews<b> 2</b>:11-12, with <i>members of </i>Christ’s<i>
family</i> in 1 Corinthians <b>2</b>:1; Philippians <b>3</b>:1; 1 Thessalonians
<b>1</b>:4; 2 Thessalonians <b>2</b>:13; <b>3</b>:15, with <i>fellow Christians</i>
in 1 Corinthians <b>6</b>:8; <b>8</b>:13; <b>15</b>:1; 2 Corinthians <b>1</b>:8;
Philippians <b>4</b>:1; 1 Thessalonians <b>4</b>:6; James <b>2</b>:1; 1 John <b>3</b>:14,
with <i>a Christian</i> in 1 Corinthians <b>8</b>:11, with <i>Christians </i>in
Colossians <b>4</b>:15; 1 John <b>3</b>:20, with <i>God’s children </i>in 1
John <b>4</b>:21<i>.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Biblical
writers always present <u>God</u> himself in masculine terms. We have
maintained that presentation because a translator’s role is to express what the
author said, not what a translator or reader might think is true. Of course,
from the perspective of canonical revelation about that truth, God is (a)
spirit (John<b> 4</b>:24) and as such does not have <i>“flesh and bones” </i>(Luke
<b>24</b>:39). So, sexuality/gender cannot belong to his ontic nature. That
fact, we surmise, lies in part behind proscribing idolatry. It also coincides
with placing final responsibility in the “hands” of the masculine and with observing
that the female is physically weaker—statistically by degree (cp. 1 Peter <b>3</b>:7:
<i>weaker vessel</i>). The God of the Bible has ultimate responsibility for the
rest of reality, hence authority over it, and ultimate power to direct it. His
omnipotence establishes stability and security and guarantees hope for his
people. The human parallel <i>in his image</i> likewise issues in a generality
where greater physical strength conveys greater psychological security. (<i>“In
his image” </i>we understand as the interpersonal capacity and therefore aside
from the human sexuality question altogether.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            Additionally,
references to <u>angels</u> are always masculine in scripture and hence in renderings
here. Angels also are “spirits” (Hebrews<b> 1</b>:14 + Luke <b>24</b>:39). With
<u>demons</u> this version nearly always uses <i>it</i> in pronoun references. That
usage fits the demons’ tendency toward depersonalizing people by the effects they
have on the ones they “possess.” Additionally, <i>demon</i> has an impersonal
feel in English even though the Greek word is grammatically masculine (</span><i><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>δαίμων</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>appearing<i>
</i>only in Matthew <b>8</b>:31). As a word, <i>Spirit</i> has a more
impersonal feel than <i>Father</i> and <i>Son</i>; perhaps that is part of the
reason for some slip-ups in some translations that render </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πνεῦμα</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(n.) as <i>“it.”</i>  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            4.
We have used <i>a</i> with a <u>noun typical of its class</u>. Older English custom
used <i>the</i>—as did Greek and Hebrew: <i>“To do his work credibly, the
artist needs to know human anatomy”</i> <i>vs.</i> <i>“To do his work credibly,
an artist needs to know human anatomy.”</i> Cp. Mathew<b> 24</b>:32; <b>25</b>:31;
Mark 1:44?; <b>7</b>:20?; Luke <b>12</b>:27; John <b>10</b>:2, 11<sup>2</sup>,
14; 1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:3; 1 Timothy<b> 3</b>:2; 4:8; James <b>5</b>:7; 1
John <b>2</b>:2. In 1 Timothy <b>5</b>:14, 18. We have even applied the
indefinite-article usage to Paul’s classic statement, <i>“I have fought the
good fight,”</i> altering it to <i>“I have fought a good fight”</i> (2 Timothy <b>4</b>:7).
<i>CV</i> has also used a plural for a noun typical of its class: <i>“so they
don’t give critics [</i></span><i><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τῷ</span></i><i><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span></i><i><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἀντικειμένῳ</span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>]
a chance to find fault” </span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Timothy <b>5</b>:14).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            5.
Although not always observed in contemporary speaking, we have sometimes retained
the <u>subjunctive mood in contrary-to-fact conditional sentences</u>: <i>“If I
were a rich man”</i> <i>vs.</i> <i>“If I was a rich man.”</i> Current use of
indicative for subjunctive works because contrary-to-fact is nearly always clear
from context and subject matter. For that reason, the New Testament writers themselves
sometimes omit the particle </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄν</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>in
the apodosis of contrary-to-fact conditional sentences. That particle
distinguishes contrary-to-fact (Type C) from “real” (Type A, also called
present or past particular conditional sentences). Omission of </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἄν</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>occurs
in Matthew <b>26</b>:24; John <b>9</b>:33; <b>15</b>:22; Acts <b>26</b>:32; 1
Corinthians <b>12</b>:17<sup>2</sup>, 19; 2 Corinthians <b>2</b>:2. We did let
our guard down in Galatians <b>1</b>:10: <i>“If I was trying to please people,
I wouldn’t be serving Christ”</i>—likewise in Luke <b>7</b>:39; <b>12</b>:49; <b>17</b>:2;
John <b>18</b>:30, 36; <b>21</b>:24; 1 Corinthians <b>12</b>:17.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            6.
Intensive pronouns appear last in their clauses—instead of after the nouns they
intensify: <i>“Jesus didn’t perform the baptisms himself; his disciples did
them”</i>; <i>vs.</i> <i>“Jesus himself didn’t perform the baptisms” </i>(John<b>
4</b>:2).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>             Intensive
pronouns have also been dropped when deemed unnecessary: 1 Thessalonians <b>1</b>:19;
2 Thessalonians <b>2</b>:16. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *7.
(a) <i>CV</i> does not capitalize <u>pronouns for deity</u> except for the indefinite
pronoun <u>One</u> when standing alone: <i>“God’s ‘bread’ is the One that comes
down from heaven”</i> (John <b>6</b>:35). Older English usage capitalized
personal pronouns for deity to show respect. Some earlier authors even
capitalized intensive, reflexive, and relative pronouns.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  
(b) For the most part, <u>descriptive nouns</u> for God in some role appear in
lower case: creator, judge, redeemer. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                 
 (c) We have regarded as titles quite a few nouns and phrases that serve as <u>names</u>
for deity and for other significant references: </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “Advocate”
(1 John<b> 2</b>:1)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “Alpha
and Omega” (Revelation <b>1</b>:8; <b>22</b>:13),</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “Apostle
and High Priest” (Hebrews<b> 3</b>:1), </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “Assassins”
(Acts<b> 21</b>:38),</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “Author”

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span

(Romans <b>3</b>:30; Ephesians <b>2</b>:11),</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “Unleavened
Bread” (Matthew <b>26</b>:17; Mark <b>14</b>:1; Luke <b>22</b>:1, 7; John <b>7</b>:2;
Acts </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    <b>12</b>:3;

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        “the
Way” (</span><b><sup><span style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>+</span></sup></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Acts<b> 9</b>:2; <b>19</b>:9,
23; <b>22</b>:4; <b>24</b>:14,<b> 22</b>; 2 Peter <b>2</b>:2)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                   
(d) Other capitalization matters: lower case: <i>law</i> (as a kind of thing)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                                    regularly
capitalized: <i>Passover</i>, <i>Sabbath</i>, <i>Savior, Son, the Law (of Moses)
</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            8.
Some <u>misplaced<i> only’s</i> and <i>just’s</i></u> appear<b>: </b><i>“You
can only serve one master” vs.</i> <i>“You can serve only one master”</i> (Matthew
<b>6</b>:24). As per standard usage, <i>only serve</i> contrasts with some
other act because it precedes the verb. But that is not the way speakers have
come to mean it. Rewording can avoid the issue in this example: <i>CV</i> says,
<i>“</i></span><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>Nobody can serve two masters.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            9.
Some <u>conjunction-preposition switches</u> have occurred: <i>than me</i>
(preposition)<i> vs</i>. <i>than I</i> (conjunction). <i>“He is taller than me”</i>
idiomatic for <i>“He is taller than I</i> (am).<i>”</i> In the last sentence,
adding <i>am</i> takes away the artificial sound. <i>Than</i>, as a
conjunction, carries the same case after as before. Used as a preposition, <i>than</i>
governs the objective case.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        <i>As</i>,
the subordinate conjunction, likewise carries the same case after as before;
that is, it has the same case on the subsequent noun or pronoun as the previous
substantive it compares with: <i>“He is as tall as I”</i>; <i>“the loss hit him
as hard as me.”</i> Considering <i>as</i> a preposition shows up when the
speaker uses objective case after it on a pronoun that pairs with a previous subjective
form—as in the first example here: <i>“He is as tall as me.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        That
switch happens as well with <i>as-like</i>: <i>“as me” </i>(preposition) <i>vs.</i>
<i>“as I”</i> (conjunction); <i>“like they are”</i> (conjunction) <i>vs.</i> <i>“like
them”</i> (preposition). Cp. <i>than</i> and <i>like</i> in the word list that
closes these notes. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        The
issue comes up only if a pronoun appears after <i>as</i>, <i>like</i>, or <i>than</i>.
Only pronouns have different subjective and objective forms. And it comes up
only if the parallels are with a previous subjective case. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            10.
CV has retained the transitive-intransitive distinction between lie-lay,
rise-raise, sit-set, even though in common speech people often say, <i>“I am going
to lay down for a nap”</i>; <i>“I’ve set here long enough”</i>; <i>“I can’t
raise up; my knee hurts.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            11.
<u>Double negative</u> for emphasis does not appear. That practice includes <i>can’t
help but</i>. Greek uses double—even triple—negatives for emphasis, but English
continues to frown on them: <i>“I’m not doing nothing, Mom!”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            *12.
<i>CV</i> has assigned pronoun forms by <u>distribution</u> more than case. If a
pronoun precedes the verb, it carries subjective form (<i>I</i>, <i>we</i>, <i>he</i>,
<i>she</i>, <i>they</i>, <i>who</i>). After the verb, it carries objective form
(<i>me</i>, <i>us</i>, <i>him</i>, <i>her</i>, <i>them</i>, [<i>whom</i>]). Since
English has lost case form on adjectives and all substantives except pronouns,
English speakers have lost an automatic feel for syntax based on form; they have
moved to syntax based on placement (distribution). They continually confuse what
form to use where. They apply the distributive pattern the same way in
sentences with linking/equative/copulative/state-of-being verbs as they do with
action-verb sentences: <i>“It is me/it’s me”</i> (Matthew<b> 14</b>:27; ASV, <i>e.g</i>.,
has <i><u>“It is I”</u></i>; literally the Greek says, <i>“I am”</i> as
comparable to <i>“It hit me”</i>; cp. John <b>21</b>:4, <i>“They didn’t know it
was him”</i>;<i> “Who Jesus meant”</i> (</span><sup><span style='font-size:
14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>+</span></sup><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John
<b>13</b>:24; cp. <b>13</b>:22). So <i>CV</i> has adopted the pattern in, <i>“Don’t
be afraid; it’s me”</i>; cp. Luke<b> 24</b>:39. <i>“It’s not me that’s living,
but Christ”</i> (Galatians <b>2</b>:20. Such wording seems appropriate enough in
oral exchange (“conversational” and “simple”). <i>“It’s not me, is it?”</i>
(Matthew <b>26</b>:22, 25 = Mark <b>14</b>:19. See also Matthew <b>26</b>:48 =
Mark <b>14</b>:44; 1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:10; <b>15</b>:10, 11; Galatians <b>2</b>:20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        In
something of the opposite direction, modern English speakers frequently misuse
pronouns in compound objects of a preposition: <i>between you and I</i> is a recurring
slip-up. Objective forms always follow prepositions in standard English—in this
illustration <i>between you and me</i>. The substitute formulation makes <i>you
and I</i> a set expression regardless of syntactical connection—objects of
prepositions or compound direct objects (Note <i>Current World Archaeology</i>
#122, p38). The situation with “<i>___ and me</i>” does come up in Matthew <b>17</b>:27
(<i>“Give it to them for you and me”</i>); John <b>2</b>:4 (<i>“What to you and
me” </i>replaced with <i>“What <u>is</u> this?”</i>); 1 Corinthians <b>4</b>:6 (<i>“I’ve
applied these matters to Apollos and me.”</i>); Philemon 11 (<i>“He’s useful to
you and me”</i>); Acts <b>15</b>:9 (<i>“He made no distinction between them and
us”</i>).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        In
some other instances, it is simply a matter of discarding grammatical niceties:
<i>“Who sinned, him or his parents?”</i> (John <b>9</b>:2) <i>vs</i>. <i>“Who
sinned, he or his parents.”</i> </span><b><sup><span style='font-size:14.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>4</span></sup></b><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>”Whoever
<u>says</u> <u>they</u> know him but <u>do</u>n’t keep his commandments, <u>is</u>
not telling the truth and <u>does</u>n’t have the truth” </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(1
John <b>2</b>:4).   </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            13.
<u>Whom(ever)</u> does not occur. The objective form <i>whom</i> hardly comes
out “natural” in modern English. It sounds pedantic, perfunctory, stilted, aloof.
Instead, <i>CV</i> uses <i>that</i> or even rewords the sentence to avoid the offending
word. In a few cases, to avoid <i>whom</i>, the wording has <i>that</i> after a
comma to begin a non-restrictive clause (even though such a construction conflicts
with the consistent use of <i>that</i> for restrictive clauses—not enclosed in
commas—<i>vs</i>. <i>which</i> for non-restrictive impersonal—enclosed in commas).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            14.
<i>CV</i> wording freely uses <u>postpositive prepositions</u>: <i>“Who did you
give the ticket to?”</i> <i>vs</i>. <i>“To whom did you give the ticket?”</i> Here,
<i>who</i> is the conceptual object of <i>to</i> in the first example, but it does
not use the standard syntactical sequence and case form to make that
connection. Note Matthew <b>11</b>:10, 27, <i>etc.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            15.
Some <u>split infinitives</u> appear because that usage is increasingly
establishing itself in modern English. The objection to split infinitives never
had any more of a real basis than split progressive verbs: “We <i>are
continually striving</i> to improve.” Latin, Greek, and Hebrew infinitives are
always one word (portare, </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>λῦειν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,</span><span
lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>קְטֹל<b>
</b></span><span dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span dir=LTR></span>), and therefore indivisible/“unsplitable”
(except for the rare possibility of a Greek periphrastic infinitive). Most of
the time, <i>to</i> precedes a verb as the “sign” of an English infinitive: “to
work.” (“Let us <i>go</i>” does not use the sign.) So, the two words in the
infinitive can be separated as they are in progressive verbs: 1 Thessalonians <b>3</b>:5,
<i>“I didn’t want your tempter <u>to</u> somehow <u>make</u> our labor be for
nothing”</i>; also Acts <b>5</b>:11; 1 John <b>1</b>:4.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            16.
In indirect discourse, <i>CV</i> puts all the indirect discourse clause after
the conjunction that introduces it—after <i>that</i>, whether expressed or
implied: <i>“I want you to know the gospel preached by me that it was not
according to man”</i> becomes <i>“the message I presented to you didn’t come
from other people”</i> (Galatians<b> 1</b>:11). Cp. John <b>4</b>:35; <b>9</b>:8;
<b>11</b>:31; 1 Corinthians <b>3</b>:20 &lt; Psalm <b>94</b>:11; <b>14</b>:57;
2 Corinthians <b>2</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            17.
In indirect discourse, <i>that</i> is not usually used to introduce the subordinate
noun clause. Jesus said, <i>“I am not going up to the feast yet”</i> could be expressed
as indirect discourse with no <i>that</i>, <i>“He said <s>that</s> he wasn’t
going up to the feast yet”</i> (cp. John<b> 7</b>:1-9)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            18.
<i>CV</i> almost always omits the relative pronoun <i>that</i> when it fills an
objective slot in a subsequent relative clause: <i>“I didn’t read the book <s>that</s>
he gave me.” </i>That arrangement can happen in combination with postpositive
prepositions: <i>“I didn’t like the school he sent me to”</i> <i>vs</i>. <i>“I
didn’t like the school to which he sent me.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            19.
<i>CV</i> generally avoids participial phrases. They sound too unusual for conversational
taste. In this work, participles have become finite verbs in relative,
adverbial, or independent clauses.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            20.
Colloquial wording has sometime replaced standard grammatical formulae as with
possessives before gerunds for agency: <i>“They resented them teaching the
people”</i> (Acts <b>4</b>:2) <i>vs</i>. standard English <i>“resented their
teaching the people”</i>; 2 Thessalonians <b>2</b>:5, <i>“me telling you” vs</i>.
<i>“my telling you”</i>; 2 Corinthians <b>13</b>:9 <i>“you being strong”</i>;
note also Matthew <b>10</b>:29; John <b>15</b>:8. The difference comes from switching
an -ing gerund to an –ing participle that looks and sounds the same. Another
way of looking at it considers it indirect discourse with a participle for its
verb. Retained possessives with gerunds include Romans <b>4</b>:5; <b>9</b>:11-12;
<b>11</b>:15<sup>2</sup>; 2 Peter <b>2</b>:12.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            21.
<i>CV</i> tries to eliminate cumbersome assonance: “those that,” “think
through.”</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            22.
In many lists, we have dropped coordinating conjunctions between their last two
entries—<i>and</i>, <i>or</i>. (If a <i>CV </i>list does use a conjunction in
that position, the “serial/Oxford comma” appears.) Omitting that coordinate
conjunction gives the list a representative feel rather than exhaustive one. New
Testament lists frequently have that “for example” character: Romans <b>1</b>:23,
30, 31. Lists are a frequent features of Paul’s writings especially, so
omitting the last <i>and</i> occurs frequently in his letters.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            23.
Predicate nouns, direct objects, objective complements, retained objects, and
indirect objects appear after the verb in sentences instead of using some kind
of inverted order: Matthew <b>6</b>:22 = Luke <b>11</b>:34. Following that plan
takes away some variety of expression, but it comes across more easily.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            24.
All three forms of Greek indirect discourse come into English with a finite verb.
Most frequently by far, Greek uses (a) </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ὅτι</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>plus
a finite verb after a leading verb of saying, the senses, and mental action. Greek
can also use (b) an infinitive or (c) a participle for the verbal in the
indirect-discourse part. English has virtually dropped those last two options. If
purpose-clause formulas replace indirect-discourse constructions—as after “asking”
or” thanking,” <i>CV </i>uses an infinitive phrase or some other format.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            25.
To reduce the seeming overuse of direct discourse, <i>CV</i> has <u>transformed
direct into indirect discourse in many cases</u>: Matthew<b> 6</b>:31; <b>9</b>:30,
34; <b>12</b>:10; <b>17</b>:20; <b>21</b>:3, 22; <b>26</b>:67; <b>27</b>:10,
64; <b>28</b>:13, 63; Mark <b>3</b>:30; <b>5</b>:8; <b>6</b>:24, 38; <b>14</b>:58;
Luke<b> 5</b>:14; <b>7</b>:13; <b>14:</b>17; <b>18</b>:3; <b>19</b>:3; <b>23</b>:30;
John<b> 1</b>:19; <b>2</b>:3; <b>3</b>:7; <b>4</b>:10, 35; <b>5</b>:11; <b>6</b>:42;<b>
9</b>:11;<b> 10</b>:36;<b> 12</b>:27; <b>14</b>:28; <b>13</b>:29; <b>15</b>:20;
<b>16</b>:5; <b>20</b>:17; <b>21</b>:12; Acts <b>16</b>:35; Colossians <b>4</b>:17;
1 John <b>5</b>:20; Revelation <b>9</b>:14. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            26.
We have not always used distinctive reflexive forms for reflexive pronoun
reference—<i>myself vs.</i> <i>me</i>: <i>“I’ve applied these matters to me and
Apollos to teach you . . .”</i> (1 Corinthians<b> 4</b>:6). Older English often
did the same thing, as in <i>“Now I lay me down to sleep.”</i> The <i>American
Standard Version </i>(1901) has Ezekiel say, <i>“The righteousness of the
righteous shall be upon him</i> [that is, <i>on himself</i>]<i>”</i> (<b>18</b>:20).
In most cases, Greek, Latin, and English calls for using <i>-self</i> pronouns
for restating the sentence subject if necessary for making the reference clear.
Where no confusion arises, speakers dispense with the difference. Using
reflexive pronouns in other than reflexive situations is a breach of standard
usage, and so is avoided in conversation as well. English speakers often use
reflexive pronouns to avoid sounding self-centered: <i>“That is an issue
between him and myself.” </i>Such a situation does not arise in the New
Testament text.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>            27.
<i>CV</i> uses Arabic numbers more frequently than style manuals normally
suggest, especially where several occur in the same context. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;
display:none'>Hebrew grandiloqu3ence,gr</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Individual
Words and Expressions</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>a                      </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(a)<i>
</i>replaces <i>the</i> with nouns typical of their class: Mark <b>4</b>:2; John
<b>10</b>:11<sup>2</sup>, 14; 1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:3; <b>12</b>:51, 21; 1
Timothy <b>3</b>:2</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
familiar case in Matthew <b>1</b>:23 bears comment. The Greek carries an
article on <i>virgin</i>: </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ἰδοὺ</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἡ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>παρθένος</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἐν</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>γαστρί</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ἕξει</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>καὶ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τέξεται</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>υἱόν</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.
The quotation comes from Isaiah <b>7</b>:14. The LXX carries the article on </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>παρθένος</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>.
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ι</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>t
does so perhaps because the Hebrew text behind it carries an article. </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Α</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>rticle
usage in Hebrew does not exactly parallel article usage in Greek, and neither
parallels article usage in English. Hebrew could use its article for something
known and definite to the writer, though not to the reader: note Genesis <b>14</b>:13.
For English purposes, the definite article implies the referent is known to
both. The inference seems to be that no English definite article belongs in
Isaiah <b>7</b>:14 because the reader of Isaiah would not know about the virgin
aside from the author’s comment.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (b)
as one translation of </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τις</span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>/</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>τι</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (c)
as an occasional translation of </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>εἷς</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(<i>one</i>):
Matthew <b>9</b>:18; <b>12:</b>11; <b>26</b>:69; Mark <b>10</b>:17.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>across
from you</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'> rephrased as <i>over there</i>: Matthew <b>21</b>:2 =
Mark <b>11</b>:2 = Luke <b>19</b>:30 (but not in Luke <b>2</b>:3)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>albeit</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>   not
used</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(is) <i>able</i>
(to): usually replaced with <i>can</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(is) <i>not able</i>
(to): usually replaced with <i>cannot</i> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>also</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>     often
replaced with <i>too</i>, <i>as well</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>all  </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  (a)
<u>often omitted</u> when apparently indicating a <u>generality</u> instead of
a uniformity—as seems to be the case in most examples. That tendency, in fact,
applies to a majority of Greek absolute and universal terms: <i>every</i>, <i>everybody</i>,
<i>all</i>, <i>nobody</i>, <i>whole</i>. </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Πᾶς</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
is greatly overused in comparison to English <i>all</i>. Instead, English
custom indicates generality by simple statement: <i>“The people shouted,
‘Crucify him.’”</i> It would not say <i>“All the people shouted”</i> (cp. Luke <b>24</b>:9).
In that respect, </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>πᾶς</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> carries the force of the
Hebrew </span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>כל</span><span dir=LTR></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>. The usage reminds us of <i>such and such</i>.  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)<i>
</i>English speakers use <i>all</i>—and universal or absolute terms
generally—as part of a construction for <u>emphasis</u>: <i>“all that fussin’
around was just to get attention.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (c)
<u>replaced with <i>every</i></u>. Everyday English prefers <i>every</i> to <i>all</i>;
it thinks of distributive plural instead of the examples as a set.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>amazed</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>  
The word translated <i>amazed</i> we have replaced with <i>surprised</i> when
the incidents seem to emphasize that what happened was unexpected: Matthew <b>19</b>:25;
<b>21</b>:20; <b>27</b>:14; Mark <b>10</b>:24, 26; <b>15</b>:4, 44, <i>etc.</i> 
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>and</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                  (a)
omitted in lists and replaced by commas. The Greek text often puts <i>and</i> (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>καί</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
between list entries, as is the case with inseparable </span><span lang=HE
dir=RTL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>וְ</span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span> (<i>and</i>) </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>in </span><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Hebrew: Acts <b>6</b>:5
(list of the seven chosen to serve table), <b>6</b>:9 (list of nationalities in
Jerusalem’s Hellenistic synagogue). (The Greek text can omit </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>καί</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>between
items in a list: Titus <b>2</b>:5.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        </span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)<i>
</i>We have omitted <i>and</i> even between the last two entries in many lists,
because New Testament lists often seem representative instead of exhaustive: 1
Corinthians <b>1</b>:30; <b>2</b>:3; <b>6</b>:9-10; 1 Peter <b>1</b>:5-8; <b>2</b>:1;
<b>4</b>:3. Paul gives other virtue, vice, qualification, and general types of
lists: Romans <b>1</b>:29-31; <b>8</b>:30, 35; <b>9</b>:4; <b>1</b><b>4</b>:17;
1 Corinthians <b>1</b>:30;<b> 6</b>:9-11;<b> 5</b>:9, 11; <b>12</b>:28; 2
Corinthians <b>6</b>:4-6; <b>12</b>:20-21; Philippians <b>4</b>:8; 1 Timothy <b>1</b>:10,
12;<b> 3</b>:2-7, 8-13; <b>6</b>:17-18; 2 Timothy <b>3</b>:10; <b>4</b>:5;
Titus <b>1</b>:6-9, 10; <b>2</b>:5; <b>3</b>:9. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (c)
omitted even between two adjectives preceding a noun: “</span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>An
evil, adulterous generation is straining to see a sign”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
instead of “<i>An evil and adulterous generation is straining to see a sign”</i>
(Matthew <b>12</b>:39; cp. <b>16</b>:4).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (d)
<i>“Jesus rebuked the demon, and it came out of him, and the child was healed
from then on.”</i> The alteration becomes <i>‘Jesus rebuked the demon, and it
came out of him. The child was healed from then on”</i> (Matthew<b> 16</b>:4).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>                        (e)
in several places translated <i>but</i> where the two linked elements actually
contrast, but the author still uses <i>and</i> (</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>καί</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>):
Matthew <b>1</b>:25; <b>11</b>:17; <b>17</b>:23; <b>20</b>:10, 19; <b>21</b>:30;
<b>26</b>:60; Luke <b>18</b>:2; <b>19</b>:48; <b>20</b>:19; <b>21</b>:18; <b>24</b>:11;
Luke <b>15</b>:16; John<b> 1</b>:6; <b>7</b>:28, 34; <b>8</b>:52;<b> 9</b>:3; <b>14</b>:22;
<b>17</b>:11; <b>20</b>:14; Acts<b> 7</b>:5, 9; <b>10</b>:38; 1 Corinthians <b>13</b>:2,
3. Adversative </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>καί</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> comes into the gospels
especially; perhaps the impulse to use it that way originated—as did several tendencies
of New Testament Greek—from the Hebrew-speaking background of its writers. The
Hebrew inseparable prefixed conjunction </span><span lang=HE dir=RTL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ו</span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span> covered both conjunctive and adversative relationships. Its
closest equivalent tended to pick up that same flexibility more often perhaps
than Greek itself would have shown.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:0in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>and
it came to pass</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> generally omitted as wordy and
unnecessary. The construction represents the Hebrew </span><span lang=HE
dir=RTL style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>ויהי
